NELAL
ROAD MAMIENANCE AND RlB IL PRJECT
1. INTRODUCTION
1.1          Nepal is located in the foothills of the Himalayas and lies between India's northern border
aid China's southern border. Its per capita income of only US$ 180 is one of the lowest in the world,
and the lowest in South Asia where the average per capita income is US$ 350. Since 1991, the
democratically elected majority Govenmnt has faced the challenge of accelerating economic growth and
development in order to meet the increasing expectations of its 19 million inhabitants, half of which live
in absolute poverty. The Government has embarked on a program of economic reforms which have led
to advances in trade and industry liberalizadon including a unified exchange rate, freeing convertibility
of the current accoum and liberalizing foreign imvestment. The Govenmment has taken initial steps to
reform the civil service and started privatization of public enterprises on a pilot scale and is formulating
a major restructuring program. The main problem, however, is that the fiscal situation remains tight,
revenue efforts are still very poor and implementaion capachies are constrained in most sectors.
1.2          The Govermment has limited capacities to deliver most basic services. In the transport
sector, a core road network is in place, but the Kingdom's difficult topography and severe monsoon rains
make inexpensive, reliable tansport difficult, and some areas are not accessible by road. The lack of
wansport links and pressure on agricultural land in the hill areas have prompted steady migration into the
Terai (plains) which is the country's primary agrcultural resource and accounts for over a third of the
popuaion. Road tramsport is predominant among transport modes and central to the Govermnent's
development strategy. The Govermment considers the proposed Road Mintenance and Rehabilitation
Project a priority to help develop mainte     implementation capacities, safeguard past investments and
realize the benefits of substantial amounts of resources already dedicated to the road network.
1. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
2.1          Nepal's transport system relies heavily on a reladvely recent but modest road network
which serves low taffic volumes. Roads are complemented with trails and civil aviation facilities that
provide access to remote and sparsely populated areas. The transport network evolved from less than
650 km of roads in the mid-fifties, mosdy motorable tracks, to a present network of 8,300 kn of roads,
7,000 km of main trails, 400 modern suspension bridges and 43 airports including 20 short take-off and
landing strips.
A. The laot Sector and the Economr
2.2          During the early stages of transport development between the 1950s and the 1970s, the
transport sector absorbed 36% of total project aid and 28% of total development expenditures. The share
of project aid and development expenditr  for transport projects was reduced as the core roads, trails
and airport networks were developed, declining to 17% and 16%, respectively, in the eighties, and to
less than 12% and 7%, respectively, in the nineties.
2.3          The transport sector accounted for 4.3% of GDP in FY 1991, a decline from nearly 7%
in FY 1981. This small share does not take full account of transport and services by truck-operators
which are often not reflected in the GDP calcultons.
2.4          Road transport is the predominant sub-sector receiving over 80 percent of total transport
public exp iur, followed by aviation with 15%.



- 2-
B.RodSco
2.5          The road network's backbone is almost completed and comprises two main axes including
the East-West Highway in the Terai and the North-South road connecting India with Cbina through
Katibmandu. The national road network comprises about 8,300 mn, including some 3,000 kIn of paved
roads. With available resources and limited maintenance capabilities, the Government maintains less than
one-fourth of the total network, mostly highways and feeder roads. Moreover, every year upgrading and
extension projects add 200 km to the nmintainable network.
2.6          The network's rapid expansion has been at the expense of rapid road datc.1oration due
to the lack of maintenance finding and capabilities. In 1992, it was estimated that 75% of the total
network was in poor to fair condition, a high percentage compared to other low-income countries where
road networks have been developed recenly. The network's poor condition, compounded by Nepal's
difficult geography and weather, has resulted in high vehicle operating costs and annualized operation
costs four to five times higher than if maintenance were adequate. The resulting high transport costs are
an important constaint to Nepal's economy.
2.7          Nepal's road sector has attracted strong donor support, mainly for new constxuction and
rehabilitatin. In 1992/93, sub-sector allocations amounted to NRs 2.9 billion (US$ 63.0 million
equivalent), of which 40% were from Govenment sources. Donor fumded activities during the last
decade include: (a) road and bridge consucton projects funded by IDA, ADB and the Goverments of
China and India. (b) road flood rebabilitation actvities funded by IDA; (c) rehabilition, improvements
and resaing funded mainly by IDA, ADB, ODA, and SDC; and (d) technical assistance funded by IDA,
AX)B, ODA and SDC. A shifi of focus towards rehabilitation and mntece activities has been favored
durng the last 5 yeas by multlaeral donors and some bilateral donors including ODA and SDC.
2.8         IDA regularly holds donor meetings on the sub-sector to coordinate approaches. A
coordinted donor approach has been developed based on three recent studies including: (a) IDA's report
on "Public Expendiures in the Road Sector' (Report No. 10988,NEP), (b) Asian Development Bank's
(ADB) detailed orgation and management study (O&M) of the road sector (drafit final report October
1992), and (c) the incepon report on th maitnance manaent system prepared by the Main
and Rehabiliionordination Unit (MRCU - August 1992), with SDC and ODA's support. Regarding
HMG resorces for the road sector, the level and composition of road user revemns has not been
addressed as part of road sector fnance initatives. Road user revenues have been growing at a steady
12% per year since PY 1985/86, and in PY 1991/92 revenues from fuel tax, custom duties and sales tax
on vehicles are estimated to represent some NRs 820 million (US$ 16.4 million equivalent) which are
part of central goverment reeus. Recent trends indicace that even though the ratio of road user
revenues to to  expendiures i very low compared to other Asian countries, they amount to more than
four times the astual m_ncem exp _ade.
C. A     n &eto
2.9          Aviation, second only to roads, is an import  mode of tansport in Nepa since mny
areas are inaccessible. A network of 43 aiports of varying stndards serves Nepal and makes many
remote areas accessible. Since 1992, private operators entered the domestic market which was previously
domnated by the national crrier Royal Nepal Airlines Corporation (RNAC).
2.10         Aviation is important for Nepal to support the country's growing tourism industry and
for internal comications, especially in the Hills and Mountis. Ka_mandu's TribhUVan Airport is
the only intational aiport, with 42 additional air-strips which include 26 short tak-off and landin
strips located in the Hills and Mountains. In geneal, conditions at these domestic air-strps are poor,
and services, especially in the Hlls and Mountains, are extremely unreliable. The Government focuses
aviaion invtmes for  gadig fachties at Tribuvan Aiport, pavmg of runways and other



improvements to the domestic air-strips and some maintenance. Large capital improvement projects have
been considered in the past to upgrade Tnbhuvan and Pokhara's airports, but these are unlikely to be
justifiable or feasible given the projected volumes of traffic.
2.11         In FY 1992/93, airport revenues amounted to more than NRs 350 million (US$ 7.0
million equivalent) while projected expenditures amounted to some NRs 460 million (US$9.2 million)
including development and recurrent expenditures. Revenues are more than six times the allocations for
recurrent expenditures. There is a need to critically review the priorities for aviation sector expenditures,
particularly in the areas of navigation aids and other aviation safety enhancements. Maintenance of
aviation facilities is presently very poor due to a lack of funding, :nd the present maintenance allocations
need to be increased at least by ten-fold from the present level of about NRs 10 million per year.
D. Trails and Suspension BIidges
2.12         In the Hills and Mountains, the trail network is extensive and well used, and for those
areas not connected to the road network human porterage remains the only form of transport. With the
development cf a road system, however, the naure of the trail network has changed, with trails no longer
providing long distance transport but feeding the hills from roadheads. With 23 out of 75 districts having
no road access and some 12 distri;cts with a single earth road, many villages are accessible only by porters
travelling on foot. The overall length of these trails is estimated to be around 15,000-20,000 kan,
including 7,000 km of main trails, out of which 5,000 km are concentrated in the three western regions.
2.13         Over time trails have been, and are continuing to be, improved by construction of
pedestrian suspension bridges over rivers and gorges, which improve accessibility and reduce distances.
Some 2,500 trail bridges exist along various routes. Of these, around 400 are modern suspension bridges
constructed by the districts and the suspension bridge division of the Ministry of Works and Transport.
The condition of these bridges is highly variable, and the older bridges are generally in poor condition.
E. The titutiona and P V            ok
2.14         The management of the transport sector is primarily at the central level with some
responsibilities expected to be grdually transferred to the district level, especially for district roads.
Roads, trails and suspension bridges are the responsibility of the Department of Roads (DOR) in the
Ministry of Works and Transport (MOWT), and aviation is the responsibility of the Department of Civil
Aviation (DCA) in the Ministry of Tourism.
2.15         The Eighth Five Year Plan (EFYP) (1993-97) aims at supporting transportation
development to enhance potential growth sectors and improve regional integration. The EFYP suggests
that a master plan is needed to identify the future national transport system based on regional equity and
growth considerations, integraing the national system with regional and international networks. The
EFYP emphasizes the following priorities: (a) provide road transport to link district headquarters with
national highways; (b) enhance nral transport with improved trails and suspension bridges; (c) complete
the East-West Highway; (d) maintain existing highways and feeder roads and establish a revolving fund
for that purpose; (e) provide access to special national development programs such as hydropower; (f)
develop railways, air transport, ropeways and waterways considering cost efficiency and tourism
potential; (g) develop urban transport; and (h) consider developing a tunnel to improve access between
Kathmandu and the Terai.
2.16         The priorities of the EFYP for the sector introduce three concepts which are fimdamental
even though they are imbedded in a statement heavily biased towards constrction. First, the necessary
links between level of service and construction and mainace activities are to be established. Second,



-4 -
environmental considerations are brought in, even though not strongly enough to weigh against the
proposed emphasis on new district roads requiring north-south connections. Third, the concept of a
sector revolving fund to support maintenance operations is introduced. The EFYP starts to address
sustainability of investments, but no attempt is made to define cleatly the sense of mission of public
agencies and the development of private sector participation in the provisior, of transportation services.
F. IJDAs Secor Eznerlence d Strateg
2.17         The proposed Project would be the fiRfh IDA transport sector operation in Nepal. In the
past IDA has financed three road projects and one flood rehabilitation project with emphasis on roads and
bridges, in addition to financing a few irrigation roads as part of agriculture projects. IDA's involvement
in the transport sector has been successfil. The projects' physical objectives have been generally met
although difficult economic conditions, political changes and limited institutional capabilities have often
caused delays.
2.18         IDA's transport sector strategy is to support policies and investments that prioritize scarce
resources on programs which are central to the attainment of the main sectoral objectives of the EFYP.
The main focus of IDA's transport strategy would be to ensure that the highest priority be given to the
following investments: completing the East-West Highway, maintaing the strategic road network,
emnphasizing the development of a farm-to-market network in support of agricultural development in the
Terai, and providing access to important national development projects. IDA's strategy would also focus
on the following policy insuments: (a) prioritizing sector expenditur  to contribute to the country's
strategy of expenditure priortization through the development of a sector-wide priority investment plan
and review process based on network-based planning and basic data considering new construction,
upgrading and rehabilitation on the basis of economic criteria; (b) insuring fimding for maintenance of
the strategic transport networks; and (c) establishing a sector expenditure ceiling based on the rolling
three-year budget review process that will start in FY 1993/94. The strategy, is elaborated in
"Expenditures in the Road Sector" and in the background papers of the "Infrastructure Development
Strategy" (Report in preparation No. 11800-NEP).
2.19         In order to insure adequate connections between Nepal's road network and the port in
Calcutta through which most goods bound for Nepal are imported, IDA is assisting HMG with the
preparation of a proposed Multimodal Transit and Trade Facilitation Project which will focus on
providing the necessary infrastructure to improve transit of Nepal's cargo through India, lower the cost
of imports, and increase the competitiveness of exports. The proposed Project would finance the
development of a broad gauge rail siding into Birgunj from Raxaul, India, a nmultimodal freight tenrinal
at Birgunj and technical assistance for trade and transit facilitation.
m. TIE ROAD SU           R
A. Road rsport and the Eoonomv
3.1          Road expitures as a proportion of GDP have been declining from levels above 3%
before 1970 to 1.6% in 1991. The trend observed is the opposite to the growth observed in public
expenditures as a proportion of GDP. Such a decrease in sctor expenditures (in relative terms) occurred
at a time when the Govemment focused on expanding the road network leaving few resources to develop
necessary capabilities to manage and operate the growing capital stock.
3.2          The vehicle fleet in Nepal is very small amounting to only 55,000 vehicles, including
some 6,000 trucks and 6,000 buses. The vehicle fleet has grown steadily at about 7% annually based
on official figures. The typical truck is the Indian TATA vehicle which carries an average load of 10
tons resulting in gross vehicle weights of 15-16 tons. The general patten of road use is one of very low
utilization, with average ammal daily traffic (AADT) on inter-urban roads ranging between 100-1,600



vehicles per day (vpd). The traffic along strategic highways includes over 67% of heavy vehicles. The
maximu MAADT observed is in the road from Katumandu to the Indian border at Birgunj with 1,600
vpd. The East-West Highway traffic volumes average AADTs of 300-1,200 vpd.
3.3          Roads in Nepal are important to all aspects of economic and social development. The
most important linkages are betwem roads and agricultural production, regional exchanges and import
flows. In particular, given thai production in Nepal is relatively dispersed and considering the high
dependency on imports, economic exchanges between the main production and consumption areas are
heavily dependent on road transport.
3.4         In the case of collector (feeder) and local access (district) r3ads, the main linkage between
road rehabiliationimdrovement and economic development is the expected increase in aericultural
production. Successful agricultural development is highly dependent on access to markets and timely
provision of inputs. The importance of adequate roads to agricultural developmen in Nepal has been
often cited as a crucial element to increase aggregate agricultuial production. Studies in Nepal confirm
that road access reduces transport costs and facilitates agricultural modernization.
3.5          In the case of arterial (highways) roads, with higher levels of traffic (i.e., above 80
vehicles per day), the maain linkage between road rehabilitation/improvement and economic development
is the expected reduction in vehicle operating costs. Estimates prepared for the Project indicate that
savings of up to 20% of vehicle operating costs could be achieved by improving a road from fair to good
condition, and 80% from poor to good condition. Considering that Nepal's highway road network (2,300
knms) accounts for about 90 percent of total vehicle-kilometers and that vehicle usage factors are low on
average, amounting some 20,000 kilometers per year, it is not disproportionate to estimate that there are
an estimated 990 million vehicle-kilometers every year. Based on this rough and imperfect assessment,
the benefits to road users isulting from improvements to 50 percent of the arterial network (i.e., 400
km of roads in fair condition, and only 600 kn in poor condition) could amount to some US$16-24
million per year.
3.6          The benefits stemming from road maintenance can be calculated as net savings in public
expenditures as a result of reduced annualized agency costs for operations and maintenance of the
stategic network and reduction of the erosion of gross fixed capital investments. "Expenditures in the
Road Sector" stated that the anulized operation costs of the road network in Nepal could be reduced
by a factor of 6 with the development and inplementtion of proper maintenance systems and by a factor
of 2 only with minimum maintenance capabilities (i.e., routine maitenance). In absolute terms, the
report indicates that safeguarding past investments through the implementation of a periodic and routine
maintenance could save some US$28 million per year (Annex 1).
3.7          The benefits from road c  tution in areas with development potential are also
associated with a reduction in transport costs which often expand the frontiers of production processes
by modifying the relative returns to labor and capital, increasing access to markets and making inputs
available. Due to variations in each region's potential, geograhical constraints, distance from the main
markets, availability of complementary infstructure, and nature of the products, the rates of return can
vary widely from case to case.
B. Road Sub-Sector Ins1 onal Eraework and Polidces
3.8          MOWT is responsible for roads and road transport. Within the MOWT, the Departnent
of Roads (DOR) MOWT is responsible for the plamning, design, construction and maintenance of the
national road network (i.e., highways. feeder and district roads), while the Department of Transport
Management (DOTM) is responsible for road transport regulations and policies. Recently enacted
legislation allows District Development Committees (DDCs) to assume responsibility for local access
roads which account for 3,000 km of classified district roads.



-6 -
3.9          The Govermnent's present strategy emphasizes network extensions to link all district
centers with the main road corridors, which has constraied the Govenument's ability for improving
mainteance capabilities. However, realizing the high costs of road deterioration, DOR and the Ministry
of Finance (MOF) are gradually starting to focus on increasing institutional, financial and private sector
capacities for periodic and routine maintenance, starting with bringing the strategic network (4,000 kan
of bighways and feeder roads) to a maintainable condition (1,500 bn or 20% of the total road network).
3.10         The MOWT and DOR successfully conducted a Policy Action Planning (PAP) Workshop
on Road Maintenance last February with MOF and the National Planning Commission (NPC). The
worksiop mcreased awareness in Nepal that the past strategy of allowing roads to deteriorate to an
unserviceable condition and then rehabilitating them is four to five times more costly than maintaining
them on a reguar basis. Following the workshop, MOWT has established the mechanism to fund
periodic, emergency and routine maintenance of the road network during FY 1993/94 (financed from toll
taxes) with ,pproved guidelines and procedures which made the existing revolving fund operational.
FurtheroriM, efforts are underway to modify the revolving fund into a Road Maintenance Fund, dedicated
to road maintenance activities by contract and financed from a share of road user charges. NPC and
MOF have agreed with the concept. DOR with donor support is developing a maintenance management
system which would provide basic inputs into the preparation of the annual mamtenance programs based
on an assessment of technical and economic criteria. The workshop also included discussions on the
nstimional strategy, considering the creation of an autonomous road authority, perhaps initially
responsible only for manaining Nepal's strategic highway network including operating toll roads. The
workshop concluded that Nepal's current administive environment isn't conducive to the creation of
an effective, truly autonomous authority, and suggested that institutional efforts should focus on ways to
strengte DOR with assistance to management through specialist advisors.
C. I)A's Road Sub-Sector Ex&Wkd       and &Sr
3.11         The proposed Project would be the fifth IDA road operation in Nepal. The first two
highway projects were satisfactorily completed but with considerable delays due to material shortages and
inefficiencies in adminisrton. Upgrading DOR's maintnance capabilities and its personnel skills and
organizational practices was not as effective as anticipated. The Third Highway - Credit 1515-NEP -
(UJS$ 47.5 million of August 1984) and Road Flood Rehabilitation Project - Credit 1922-NEP - (US$ 15.5
million of May 1988) were delayed due to the 1989 trade and transit impasse with India and the
subsequent political changes in the country. Although the 3rd Highway Project has fallen four years
behind schedule, physical implemention has been generally good and quality high, and the project was
essentially completed in December 31, 1993 with additional flood related repairs pending. Training of
heavy equipment mechanics and other persomel began under the Second Highway Project - Credit 730-
NEP - and has continued satisfactorily during this project. Tihe Road Flood Rehabilitation Project -
Credit 1922-NEP - is expected to meet its objectives with a two year implementation delay. Past projects
embodied a strategy of support for construction, inadequate attention to maintenance, and necessity of
rehabilitation. While constmution and rehabilitation targets were achieved, usually with delay,
maintenance capacity was not increased. Other recurrent problems relate to the ineffective project
management and momitoring. The proposed Project aims at supporting institutional strengthening and
hunan resource development, and improving the technical and managerial skills of counterpart staff
through extensive training and technical assistance. The increasing demands for maintenance funding
resulting from IDA and other donor-supported road projects indicate that a coordinated road policy
framework is now necessary to ensure the sustainability of past investments.
3.12         IDA's strategy in Nepal is to support expenditure prioritization to encourage optimal use
of scarce public resources. The strategy for the road sector has been developed in "Expenditures in the
Road Sectora which idenified four main sector constaints including: (a) lack of network-based planning,
programmg and budgeting, (b) inadequate allocations for mantenan  and constraints affecting fiun
release procedures, (c) institutional and human resource related constraints, and (d) limited absorptive

shifting resou   allocaion in the sector to develop maintenance capabilities; (b) completing essential on-

between Katmandu and the Teral; and (e) improving trails and suspension bridges in the hills. The
increse in maintance expendur  and capabilities could be done by developing a timely, reliable and
adequate funding mechnis  for road mainteon , and concentrtng DOR's capabilities on the trunk
road network (i.e., highways and feeder roads) and DDCs' responsibilities for district roads.
3.13         The strategy suggests actions in four areas including: (a) developing network-based

roads. The total network includes 3,000 km of paved roads, 2,200 knm of gravel roads and 3,100 kn of
earth roads.

3.19         The distibution of resources between central and regional offices, as well as among
regions, indicates important recent trends as follows: (a) Increasing district level egmenditures. Even
though central expenditur  predominate (over 90%), there is a noticeable increase in district level
allocations. District level expendit   also display higher levels of budget performance. This trend
would inevitably result in a wider spread of road construction and mntenance activities. Accompanying
the above trend, the plan proposed for the coming five years includes a shift favoring reduced allocations
for arterial and collector roads and increased allocations for local access roads. The FY 1993/94 budget
increases grants transferred directly to District Development Committees by 80% by supporting direct
trasfer of 75% of the realized land tax; (b) Regional dispersion of construction activities. Road
expenditure on constuction have traditionally been concentrated in a few areas of the Hills and Terai
in Easter, Central and Western Nepal. Recent trends indicate increasing emphasis on rehabilitadon of
exstig roads (in the areas tradidtonally favored for construction) and increased construction in the
outying areas, in particular Hill and Mountain ecological zones; and (c) Shift from construction to
oehabfitation. There is a noticeable trend towards increasing rehabilitation which has become necessary
de to the neglect of maitnance actvities. As pointed out above, life-cycle costs for roads which
require rehabilitation due to indequate maintenc are up to 6 times as high as applying recurrent
routine maintenance.
3.20          Under a tight budgetary constraint, the policy followed by HMG has been to spend the
same insuicient amount every year, with a reduction in allocations in real terms for periodic and routine
maintnan. This practice is not rommended. Under a tight budgetary constraint, on the contrary,
the impact of expenditres could be increased by focusing on what can be done to maximize and optimize
the use of available resources, e.g., mantaining a strategic network and reducing the emphasis on low
priority new construction. In economic terms, this means focusing on the highest economic returns to
al expndt.
3.21         Road rehabilitation and maitenance policies have now been defined to cover the essential
backlog of rehabilitation, and periodic, routine and emergency maintenance of the strategic network.
Considering the present condition of the network, the strategy for the network would be to:
(a)   rehabilitate about 300 km of highways and 130 km of feeder roads of the
stategic road networkwhich are in fair to poor condition, through a combination
of overlays and minor improvements (highways) and surface treatent and
gravelling (feeder roads);
(b)    gradually increase the capacity to carty out periodic mainenance (i.e.,
resurbcing, gravelling and sealing) of the strategic network in poor to fair



- 9 -
condition to avoid thdei reaching the poor condition, including about 650 kIn in
the next four years; and
(c)   cury out routine mWntenpam s on tie strategic network in good and fair
condition, stegdth     operations at the Division level (a Division covers
several polil disticts), and develop the capacity to prepare for emergency
repairs during the monsoon period.
Under this strategy, the maitean  bacldog of the strategic road network would be addrsed during
the Project. The level of resources needed to address the backlog of the strategic network over the next
four years (i.e., for additional rehabilitation and maintenance) is estimated at about US$ 135 million.
The total requrements for the road setor in the first rolling three year-plan are estimated to average
about US$ 70 million per year, startng at about US$ 55 million in FY 1993/94 which is conse
with sectoral budget allocations. Reducing the mainnane bacldog would result in total anmnal benefits
amounting at least US$ 40 million per year.
F. Rd 
3.22         Road user revenu  are an important but decreasing source of Govemment revenue
amountig to some 6 percent of total revenues. Road user revemnes in Nepal, i.e., fuel and vehicle
red  taxes, are not earmarkd, and their level and composition have not been addressed as part of road
sector finance inis. Recent trends, however, indicate that even though the ratio of road user
revenues to  d exN_diures is very low compared to other countries in the region, they amounted to
more than four times the actual m expendures during the period. Esdmates show that road
user revenues are sufficient to cover the estimated short-run marginal costs of road network operaion.
In the medium term, the struture of road user revemns should be addressed to encourage efficient fleet
composition and utilizaton, as well as to provide sufficient and timely resources for road maintenance.
Road sector revenues are now increasing at a pace  m_e with inflaton, well below the real
average  owth rate of total Govemnent revenues, and below expected marginal incrses in road
operations' costs.

F. Road nawg and Bhdggng
3.24         The planning and budgedng proosses in the road sector have serious deficiencies.
3.25         lack of Network-Based Planning.  Road sector resource allocations reveal an
ovewhelming emphasis on new cotuction with contnuos under-funding of mateance budgets.
Politically mandaed priorities rather hm economic efficiency considerations cloud the rational allocation
of resources. Under-finding of on-going and new contuctionprojects reflects the absence of a planning
approach which has firther weakened past ilStitution building efforts and reduced the value of
investmens. Past road sector plans have been very ambitious and unealitic in the context of the limited
institutional capabilities and resource availabit. Resource allocation in the road sector needs to de-
emphasize new  constuction and accord priority to safeguard   past investments and developing
institutional capabilities to manage the road networL The Eighth Five Year Plan (EFYP) includes some
posive develoments in this direction. The sector objective in the future could be to minimize national
transport costs over time (considering road expenditu  plus vehicle operatng costs to road users).
3.26                    . Spficatprogress has been made during Project preparation to prioritize
sector expendtur, including definition by DOR of a strategic network, prioridzation of maintenance



- 10-
activities in roads of the strategic network based on economic criteria with available data, and
development of a network analysis in each district to assemble pre-feasibility level data on potentially
viable new construction projects in each district. Addressing priority maintenance of strategic roads
under the proposed Project by guaranteeing funding and exandig implementation capabilities during
the next four years would be a major achieement in the prioritization of sector expenditures. In an effort
to start the process of prioritization, HMG has indicated in the budget for FY 1993194 that new project
starts would be limited. HMG is also undeaking a sector by sector prioritization by NPC, now under
discussion with the line Ministries. A roUling first three-year program for FY 1994/95 would be prepared
by HMG to be discussed with IDA before April 1994. T'he highest priority in the road sector would need
to be given to incomplete and on-going new construction, rehabilitation and maintenance based on
network needs. The next step, agreed during appraisal and confirmed during negotiations with the
Government, would be the development of a Priority Investment Plan (PIP) and a review process,
covering construction and maintenance in the transport sector.2 The Govenment has agreed to support
the preparation of a PIP, with an emphasis on developing planning capabilities in DOR and NPC and
ensuring adequate participation of local groups and specialists.
3.27         Inadequate Bud=eina. Budget preparation for the road sector in Nepal could be improved
considerably. The Government expenditures are not defined and classified in terms of road projects'
objectives or of inputs and outputs. The budget does not even differentiate emergency and recurrent
mainnance within the routine   tennce budget. In fact, the budget format (project proforma) appers
to be more detailed than necessary to secure approval and is used as a project work plan.
3.28         Due to limited institutional capabilities, lack of systemic data collection (i.e., road
condition surveys, traffic counts and unit costs) and the strong influence of seasonality, mainna
budgeting processes in Nepal remains haphazard. Regardin routine and emerMencv maintenance, there
is no systematic budget exercise followed to estmaterequirements. A dependable maintenance budgetg
procedure is now being developed in DOR by the Maitenane Rehabilitation Coordination Unit (MRCU)
with donor support (ODA and SDC). MRCU is preparing the basis to esmate the needs for routine,
recurrent and emergency mantenae, involving DOR engineers at the district, regional and hedquarts
level. Division level improvements on budgeting and operations are being introduced division-by-division
as part of the Strengthened Mainteance Division (SMD) program funded by SDC and HMG. Annual
budget estimates would be prepared by DOR's Planming Unit/Data Cell based on condition surveys,
network-based priorities, traffic volumes, road location and seasonal distribution of activities. A clear
defition of responsibilities for roufine, recurrent and emergency maitnance budgeting would emerge
gradually as a consensus on the revised appropriate standards, average productivities, budget preparation
methodology, and performance meures is developed. Regardig periodic maintenance budgeting, it
only amounts to an aggregation of project specific requirements without adequate consideration of
network-based requirements, much less of resource availability. Project preparation has focused on
network-based planning to improve resource allocation for periodic maintenance. The budgeting of
periodic maintance activities would be revamped as MRCU implements the necessary data collection
and analysis based on a simple maintenc management system to set priorities, define levels of
intervention (by type of road, traffic levels and location), and set periodic maintenance standards (i.e.,
technical standards and periodicity for each activity). Such a system is expected to focus on the strategic
network in a mainnable condition.
3.29         Issues in Budget Execution. Efforts are required to reduce delays that result from
procedural, regulatory and administrative constraints. The Project needs corrective actions to improve,
streamline and rationalize fund releases to guarantee that Project benefits materialize.
3.30         Lack of Performance Budgeting. In the past, the absence of planning and the problems
with budgeting have redu    the possible linkges between planning and budgeting exercises. Efficient

3.31         Rehabilitaton and periodic mtenance activities are executed by contract, but routine
mantnance activities are generally executed by force account and are very inefficient. Realizing the
low-impact of force account opeatons, DOR is endorsing a policy of introducing on a pflot basis routine
maintenane by contract. In July 1993, the MRCU with SDC support started a pilot routine maintenance
effort in three Strengthened Maintena    Divisions (SMDs) (i.e., two SMDs including Chitwan, Dhading
and Lalitpur disticts which inctude the recently completed Naubise-Mugling Road financed under Cr.
1515-NEP) which will experiment with alternative ways of organizing routine maintance (i.e.,
combinations of force account and maintenance on contract). The experiment will include lmited
technical assistance to the divisions and logistc support and would introduce improved financial
management and internal accounting for routne maintenance. The introduction of routine maintenance
by contact based on the expeiences of the 2 SMDs would be expaded gradually to about 14 districts
during the Project (pam. 4.21).
3.32         Mai        practies in Nepal are very costly and inefficient. Existing maitenance
operations and management suffer from: (a) the lack of maintenance planning and a unified set of
standards for the county, (b) the absece of quality control in a large proportion of locally funded
operations, (c) weak denaton and reporting, (d) lack of adequate supvision of road minnance
conacts, and (e) lack of equipment to supplement small contractor capacities.
3.33         The lack of maitenance planning has long been a constraint to prepare maintenance
strategies, multi-year and yearly mainance programs under a budget constrait. The MRCU is
grdualy developing the capacity in DOR to prepare such program, by developing: (a) a baseline survey
of the network and review system (i.e., establishing a network planning cell, a central road reference
datase, conducting a pavement condition survey stem and a traffic inventory system); (b) developing
a simplified system in-ose to prepare amuial work programs; (c) developing procedures for storing and
processing the network and road specific data; and (d) monitoring maintence costs and procedres.
3.34         The preparaion studies for the Project have developed a set of standard specifications for
periodic maitenance operations. DOR/MRCU are presently reviewing the Nepal Road Sttndards and
expect to issue the document iluding maintenance standards before May 1994. DOR has taken the
initiative of including laboratory services in the forms of agreement with supervision consultants to
encourage the development of private laboraory facilities. In order to address the remaining constraints
affectig mai        operations, the Project would need to incorporate quality control guidelines,
simplify mana              on and reporting (a maintenance manual is under preparation and would
need to be implemented), and increase incentives for improved supervision by insuring that adequate and
timely resources are made available to hire consultants as engineer's reprentatves to supervise
matenac on contract. Also, efforts are needed to increase the commercial oriention of existing
public-owned equipment by consolidatng the existing plant pool in operable condition, supplementing
the fleet with appropriate small equipment and improving management of DOR's fleet for hire.
EL rd d T
3.35         DOR employs about 3,000 staff, including personnel attached from the MOF and MOGA.
The employee to network ratio (about one employee per three kilometers) is in line with other countries
where labor-based methods are employed. However, the number of professionals at about 650 is high
compared to the total work force. DOR employs over 10 percent of the engineers in Nepal. Of Nepal's



- 12 -
2,865 engineers in 1992, DOR employed 346 (including temporary staff) until November 1992, when
32 engineers Class I were reired from the civil service, leaving only 6 engineers Class I in DOR's
management. Overall, there are few inctives to attract, retain and motivate experienced staff to join
both DOR and DOTM. One of the few ways to retain the most experienced engineers available to DOR
is to assign them as seconded staff to participate in donor-funded projects and to develop training
programs associated with career development.
3.36         During Project preparation, MOWT and DOR staff have emphasized the need for a
comprehensive training program to support human resource development and improve the technical and
managerial skills of counterpart staff. The main training needs identified during Project preparation
include: (i) training DOR's management in planning, programming and budgeting; (ii) training DOR's
engieers and contractors in road maenance operations, contract management and supervision, and
monitoring; (iii) contiue on-going training of equipment mechanics, operators and laboratory technicians
(DOR and private sector); and (iv) training DDCs' staff and contractors in labor-based methods for
district road rehabilitation. During appraisal, agreement was reached between HMG/IDA/ODA/SDC on
the human resources and institutional development strategy for the next four years which would focus on
strengthening the operational capacity of DOR (details below in the sector strategy).
I. EnLmnt  Saferu&ds
3.37         DOR has established an environmental unit in the Planning Section to develop
environmental assessment mainly of new construction projects. During Project preparation, a Sectoral
Envirnental Assessment (SEA) has been undertake to develop a simple, standard and practical
methodology to assess environmet  considerations during the design and construction of all DOR
projects. DOR has agreed to implement the methodology initially for screening of maintenance contracts
funded under the Project and to incorporate in Project documents and engineering designs the specific
clauses recommended i the SEA to ensure environmental safeguards in respect of: (a) landscaping
borrow areas, quarries and spoil tips; (b) storage areas and especially those for diesel fuel and bitumen;
(c) for office yards to avoid groundwater and stream pollution; and (d) slope stabilization. The Project
would include an action plan to insure adequate support to incorporate the SEA into DOR's project
appraisal procedures. (para. 4.34)
J. Sector Strateafv
3.38         The report "Public Expenditur  in the Road Sector" identified four main sector
constraints including: (a) lack of network-based planning, programming and budgeting, (b) inadequate
allocations for maitenan  and const     affecting fimd release procedures, (c) institutional and human
resource related constraints, and (d) limited absorptive capacities affecting operations. The recommended
sector straeg accords the highest priority to: (a) shifting resource allocation in the sector to develop
maintenance capabilities; (b) completing essential on-going new construction; (c) developing farm-to-
market roads in the Terai; (d) improving accessibility between Kathmandu and the Terai; and (e)
improving trails and suspension bridges in the hills. The increase in maintenance expenditures and
capabilities could be done by developing a timely, reliable and adequate funding mechanism for road
maintenance, and conceting DOR's capabilities on the trunk road network (i.e., highways and feeder
roads) and DDCs' responsibilities for district roads. The strategy suggests actions on four areas
including: (a) developing network-based planning, improving collection and disbursement of funds and
introducing performance budgeting; (b) insuring adequate, timely funding for road maintenance; (c)
increasing efforts and codiatin  itiatives that support human resource and institutional development;
and (d) increasing the capacity of domestic contractors, improving maintenance contract management
(e.g., adoption of appropiae standards, introduction of inproved quality control mechanisms), and
itoducng routine mantenan  on contract (to replace force account).

3.39         The human resources and institutionaldevelopnent (HRID) strategy agreed for the Project
would emphasize human resource development efforts to encourage DoR's staff, contractors and
consultants to acquire and develop needed skills in road sector operations and development through
focused short and long-enn traiinig inputs, and institutional strengthening efforts to enable DOR to
manage resources more efficiently within a chaging emniromnent. The HRID strategy builds upon the
O&M report and on-going training initiatives and consists of short and long-term objectives. In the short
term, the main objectives would be to: establish a focal point in DOR to coordinate and manage road
sector training; establish and organize on-the-job training for DOR's staff and contractors; assist DOR
with the establishment of regional level implementation capacities for periodic and routine road
maintenance; and assist DOR to encourage better management practices. The associated short term
training inputs would include: specialist inputs to coordinate training; on-the-job training for DOR's
technical staff and local contractors in budgeting, supervision of works, quality control, contract
management md procurement related to periodic maintenance operations; specific training for DOR's
managers; and a small training/study exercise to include visits by a few key Nepalese officials to other
countries to assess institutional options to manage road networks including road authorities and
government departments. The IOg term objectives would include: (a) supporting the completion of a
road maintenance infrmation system in DOR/MRCU; (b) supportng the development of planning
capabilities in NPC's transport unit and DOR; (c) condmied support for the Mechamics and Operators
Training Workshop including training of trainers, redesigmng and improving training programs to attract
senior mechanics, laboratory technicians and workshop supervisors, providing necessary machine tools
and laboratory eunent, and ensuring adequate mnenance and operation of the training facilities; (d)
trainig of DDCs staff and contractors in labor-based methods for district road rehabilitation; (e) bridge
management (i.e., road and trail bridges); (f) geotechnical and environmental engineering to establish
sound road side support practies in DOR including implementation of the sector environmental
assessment; (g) improve plant opeaions increasing the commercial orientation of the heavy equipment
divisions; and (h) road safety to develop and support a national road safety strategy.
IV. I= PROJECT
A. Proiect Orig-. Obectives and Rationale
4.1          ho=       i   The Project's concept developed as a result of the experience from
previous IDA-financed road sector operations which highlighted the need to develop maintenance
capabilities, the county's strategy of improving the efficiency of public expenditures and hence
prioritization, and the re-activation of efforts to decentralize some administrative functions of Government
which would allow DOR to focus on the stegic road network. The Project strategy supports the sector
strategy developed in wExpen   in the Road Sector'.
4.2          P_roject Obqjtives. The primary objective of the Project is to address the development
of road mannce capabilities and support maintenance and rehabilitation activities, by: (a) insuring
adequate funding of priority m ai     actvities by establishing fumding and budgeting arrangements
which would chamel a much greater, timely flow of funds to support maintenance within sectoral
allocation ceilings based on a rolling 3-year priority investment program to be revised annually and
reviewed with IDA; (b) strengthening sectoral planning by introducing network-based plamning, initially
to prioritize maintenance and teafter to support the development of a Priority Investment Plan (PIP)
and review process for all sector investments; (c) inproving public sector implementation capacity to
expand the coverage and enbance the quality of maintnance with a program of institutional strengthening
and human resource development based primarily on training and technical assistance inputs (establish
regional maintenance capabiLities, coordinate and develop a comprehensive training program for DOR's
staf  and increasing incentives to DOR staff with increased funding levels for road mintenance,
enhanced quality control, and introduction of performance targeting; (d) supporting development of
private sector capabilities to do mantenance on contract by supporting on-the-job training, improving
procurem  of maitenance contracts, gradually developing a critical mass of yearly or multi-yearly



- 14-
ma_ntnan  conacs, streamd_ning contract supervision and insuring timely and regulr fumding and
payments; and (d) testing ways to develop DDCs' road matenance capabilities for local access roads
(i.e., distict roads) through a pilot project. In general, the Project would be aimed at reducing vehicle
operat  costs and the delays associated with a deteriorated road system and generating much-needed
rura employment trough adoption of labor-based technologies (appropriate and efficient combination
of existing capital or labor intensive techniques).
4.3          Rationale for IDA Involvement. IDA's involvement would support an overhaul of road
administrtion and maintenance  d contribute to the country's strategy of expenditure prioritization to
encourage optimal use of scarce public resources. IDA's involvement would strengthen the process of
institution building and foster broad-based donor support for a common program of mainten     and
rehabilitation. The Project would be a deparure from past projects in which the core was a program of
selected road investments, complemented with institutional reforms that were incremental in nature. In
conrast, the Project would emphasize the development of permanent institutional capacity (for planning,
programing, budgeting design, construction and mantenance), supplemented with support for essent_i
investments in physical works.

(b) M_ainenance and Rehabilitton Progms
4.12         The naintenance components to be financed by IDA would include: (a) support for a
backlog periodic maintenance program of roads and bridges of the strategic road network in the Central,
Western, Mid-Western and Far-Western regions including about 500 km of resealing, 150 km of
gravelling and sealing, and minor bridge repairs (21% of the Project cost); (b) rehabilitation and over'ays
of 300 km of selected highways and 130 kn of feeder roads of the strategic road network (27%); and
(c) a pilot labor-based district road rehabilitation and maintenance component to support rehabilitation
and maintenance of 140 kan in 4 selected districts and promotion of app:opriate rural transport technology
to encourage community participation in rvad maintenance and related activities (4.4%). The Government
would fund the gradual development of a comprehensive routine maintenance program by contract
(7.9%).
4.13         In the course of Project preparation, DOR with assistance from specialists, has identified
the rehabilitation activities and the program of maintenance activities on the basis of the Bank's Highway
Design and Mainteance Standards Model (HDM-I), economic criteria, and the available data base.
The maintenance strategy for the strategic network (4,000 km of highways and feeder roads)
recommends: (a) pavement rehabilitation of road sections that have reached or exceeded their lifetime
tirough asphalt overlays; (b) resurfacing works of paved road sections which would otherwise require
extensive rehabilitation through surface treatment or thin asphalt overlays depending on traffic levels (or
regravelling in the case of gravel surface road sections and sealing those sections where it is economically
feasible); (c) routine maitenance works including local repair of roadway and pavement, grading of
unpaved surfaces and shoulders, regular mainnce of road drainage, side slopes, verges, traffic control
devices, and furniture, roadside cleaning, dust and vegetation control. and maintaining safety
appurtenances; and (d) emergency maintenance works including clearing landslides, river training works
and repair of drainage structhres. The rehabilitation component would cover the needs of the strategic
highway network. The surface treatments recommended in the multi-year program of resealing,
gravelling and sealing is based on data available at appraisal and is expected to be refined and reviewed
anually as analytical capabilities are inproved in DOR/MRCU and the maitenance information systems
are implemented.
4.14         Rehabilitation of 300 km of Hifhwavs and 130 km of Feeder Roads. (Map No. IBRD
24777) A program of rehbilitation of 300 km of strategic highways was prepared in the course of
Project preparation based on an initia prioritization by DORIMRCU, followed by detailed surveys,
economic analysis and an application of the HDM-l model adapted to local conditions. A limnited
program of rehabilitation of 130 km of strategic feeder roads was recommended for inclusion in the
rebabilitationprogramby NPC, which was further analyzed based on detailed surveys, economic analysis
and an application of the HDM-M model. The detailed field surveys indicate that all highway and feeder
road sections selected are in poor condition, with high indices of road roughness (International Roughness
Index (IR1) of roughness averaging somewhere between 7.0 to 12.6 mi/kn) in most cases. Severe
pavement distress were found to be frequent. The rehabilitation program was developed comparing
alternative appropriate tecbniques (ranging from asphalt overlays to complete reconstruction of pavement,
or regravelling) and embodies an optimm rehabilitation and maintenance strategy including mostly
asphaltic overlays for strengthening, drainage improvements and minor widenings, or regravelling to be
carried out during FY 94-97. The rehabilitation component of the Project includes six sections of
strategic highways and three sections of strategic feeder roads in the Central and Western regions (Annex
4). This component includes civil works (base costs of US$ 22.8 million) and construction supervision
(base costs US$ 2.2 million).
4.15         Support for Periodic Mainta   of Roads and Bridges. A prioritization of the strategic
network periodic mantnce was conducted based on available data. A periodic maintenance program
was prepared based on pavement condition surveys, economic analyses and use of the HDM-Il model.
The periodic maintenan  program includes surface treaunents (single or double) for paved sections and

gravelling and sealing of unpaved sections of the strategic highway network. (See Map IBRD No. 24779).
The periodic maintenance program to adress the needs of the strategic network between FY 95-98 and
agreed with NPC and DOR includes resealing of 500 km and gravelling ad sealing of 150 kan of roads
in Central, Western, Mid-Westen and Far-Western regions of Nepal. The scope of the annual
maintenmce programs, as deailed in Annex 3, would increase gradually to a level commensurate with
the average annual requirements of the network. The program includes 114 kn in FY 94/95, 160 kan
in FY 95/96, 175 km in FY 96/97 and 160 mn in FY 97198. In the course of preparation, detailed
surveys, economic analyses, and bidding documents have been prepared for the first year periodic
mainance program.

4.18         Expansion of DOR's maintenance capabilities to the regions/divisions, especially to

assistance in the preparation of annual maintnance programs in each region based on the central and
regional level priorities established by the DOR/MRCU.

4.21         Routine Maintenance. The exclusive relie on force account for routine maintenance
should be discourged during the Project. It was agreed that the introduction of routine maintenance by

4.22         Pilot Labor^Based District Road Rehabilitation and Mn ce Project. The main
objectives of this pilot component would be to: (a) develop small enterprises (contractors) to undertake
road rehabilitation and maitnc works with appropriate labor-based techniques involving trained labor
and small equipmt in lieu of the traditional heavy equipment; (b) develop sustainable local skills in rural
areas in low-cost road mantnance which in the future can be contracted by local agencies in the routine
mainenance of rural roads; (c) develop commuity participation in infrastructure maintenance; (d)
promote appropriate rural transport technology to make effective use of road investments; (e) support the
gradual transfer of responsibilities for district roads in four pilot districts to the concemed DDCs and
develop a sustainable institutional frameworkc for district road operations as a pilot program; and (f)
prepare the way for a future integrated rural transport project.
4.23         The pilot labor-based Project cmponent would support the rehabilitation and maitenance
of about 140 km of district roads (See Map IBRD No. 24778), procure about 8 sets of small equipment
to regravell district roads and vehicles for supervision, and develop a training program for small
enterprises (contractors) and road users groups in labor-based road rehabilitation. This component
includes civil works, equipment and vehicles (i.e., 2 four-wheel drive (DRSDU), 4 single-cabin pickups
(1 for each DDC engineer) and 8 motorcycles for the overseer staff (DDC) involved in supervision) to
be financed by IDA (base cost US$ 3.1 million), consulting services for supervision to be financed by
IDA (US$ 0.15 million) and tecenical assistance and training to be financed by UNDP (US$1 million).
In addition, the pilot project will have a small componet to introduce suitable vehicle adaptations for
rural transport, particularly promotion of low-cost 'bicycle trailers' which can be .sily attached to (or
deached from) bicycles to carry goods in the load-range of 100- 150 kgs on rural roads; there is presently
no appropriate rural vehicle for this parcel size in Nepal, between the bicycle carrying about 50 kgs and
a tracw or cart carryi    1 to 2 tons (US$ 0.025 million). These trailers can also be pushed or pulled
as carts in lieu of head-poterage; some adaptations can also be used as "rural ambulances" to carry sick
people to rral health centers, targeted to assist the poor.



- 19 -
4.24           The main features of tbis Project component are the appropriate mix of small equipment
with labor and the introduction of training for contractors and supervisors in improved management
techniques. This is a departure from the traditional method of using expensive heavy equipment or large
labor gangs in labor-intensive works without proper management or training. The result of the new
method will be lower unit costs for road rehabilitation, encouragement to the road contracting industry,
increased employment (each contractor with about US$ 80,000 worth of equipment employing about 120
people daily producing about 2 kn of high quality road rehabilitation every month), and an improved
rural economy. The pilot labor-based Project component is based on successful experience in many other
countries.
4.25           The scope of the rehabilitation program  under this component, agreed to during
negotiations, includes 140 km of district roads which have been selected based consultation with DDCs,
simplified pre-screening based on criteria for suitability for the progran, social and economic factors,
and economic feasibility analyses. (Annex 6) During preparation detailed design and final feasibility
analyses were completed for 40 km. During negotiations it was agreed that final feasibility and
environmental screening for the remnng 100 km selected would be prepared during implementation.

4.29         HMG's share of the total Project cost would include about US$ 1.9 million in recurrent
costs and US$ 6.4 million in routine maintenance. As mentioned above, in order to ensure adequate,
timely and reliable funding for road maintenance from HMG, the Project has two main policy initiatives
including: (a) establishment of a road maintenance funding mechanism, and (b) agreement on annual
allocations for road sector expenditu  (including maintenance) and annual targets for maintenance
funding based on a rolling 3-year plan. The implications for Project financing are described belcw.
4.30         Road Maintence nam ding Mechanism. DOR and MOF have increased allocations for
road maintenance in the budget for FY 1993/94 to NRs 230 million for recurrent and routine
maintenance. Following the approval of guidelines and procedures for the revolving fund based on toll
taxes MOP, a mechanism has been established to provide timely, reliable funds for road maintenance.
DurM negotiations, ageements were reached on the timetable to expand the revolving fund concept into
a Road Maintenance Fund during Project implementation. The MOWT has already prepared a draft Bill
for the Road Maintenance Fund, to be considered by Parliament before March 31, 1994. During
MgW_Uons, the Government agreed to further develop the guidelines and procedures (already approved
for the revolving fund) to support the functioning of the Road Mainenance Fund once it is established
(on or about July 31, 1994); NPC has already prepared a draft of the expanded guidelines and
procedures for the satisfactory operation of the Fund, which have been forwarded for IDA's review and
comments before negodadons. Agreements were reached with EMG durine negotiations on: (a)
establishnt of the Road Ma_intnance Fund during FY 1994/95; (b) onfrmation that maintenance
would be financed from a share of road user charges; (c) confinnation that m ance allocations will
be transferred regularly to a DOR account for payments against maintenance contracts; (d) agreement to
have quarterly estimates of Project and global mamienance expenditures in periodic and routine
mntenance activities; (e) agreement to conduct an operational audit of maintenance activities in 1995
and 1997; and (e) agreement that counterpart funding requirements for the Project would be estimated
quarterly and met promptly.
4.31         Annual Sectoral Ceiings and Maintenance TarWets. In FY 1991/92, road sector
allocations and expndiurs amounted to NRs 2.5 and 2.2 billion (US$ 50 and 44 million equivalent)
respectively, including 38% of exenditures for rehabilitation and 6% for maintenance. In FY 1992/93,
road sector allocations and estimated expenditures anunted to NRs 2.9 and 2.4 billion (US$ 63 and 52
million equivalent), respectively, including only 3% for maintenance. Allocations for FY 1993/94 are
estimated at about NRs 2.5 billion (US$ 51.0 million equivalent). Agreenent was reached during
ngotia   with HMG on road sector allocation and expenditure ceilings averaging about US$ 70 million
equivalent over the next three years, with a share (increasing from 10% to 15%) earmarked for periodic
and routine road minenance. Agreme  was reached with HMG on annual budgetary allocations for
routine and periodic madntenance as detailed in Annex 9.

D. ProLect Environmental Imact
4.34         The Project is not likely to have any significant adverse effects on the environment.
Existing road alignments will be followed to the extent technically feasible, and any deviations are
expected to be minor. A Sectoral Environental Assessment (SEA) has been undertaken to develop a
simple, stndard and practical screening of Project components, every year of the Project for review by
IDA, with a view to strengthen environnental considerations in the design and construction stages of all
DOR projects (Annex 13). DOR would establish an environmental unit and appoint a senior engineer
to head the unit witbin its Planning Section, by May 31, 1994, date after wbich the sector environmental
action plan would start being implemented. Provisions will be incorporated in design and bidding
documents. It was agreed during negotiations with HMG that the SEA would be adopted by March 31,
1994, for purposes of screening the road programs of each years program. The environmental unit would
conduct an environmenl screening of each annul periodic road maintenance program, according to the
SEA Guidelines, and a specific screening of the three feeder roads included in phase 2 of the
rehabilitation program and the Galyang-Chapakot road in the pilot labor-based component, for review by
iDA before requesting the consultants to start preparing detailed feasibility and designs.
E. Eco_noc Evaluaton and Sensitivity Analvsis
4.35         The main objective of the Project is to reduce the total cost of road transport in Nepal's
strategic road network. The economic evaluation was prepared for four separate components: 300 kn
of rehabilitation of strategic highways, 130 kn of rehabiltation of strategic feeder roads, 650 km of
periodic mantenan   of strategic highways and feeder roads, 140 kan of rebabilitation of district roads.
4.36         Ptoject Benefits. The Project's main benefit would be more efficient, cost effective
administation of the road transport system, prioritization of sector expenditures and reduction of transport
costs. Strengthening DOR would create a central focal point which will offer technical support and
assistance to local authorities in the future. The benefits of a road improvement component under the
Project consists of savings in vehicle operating costs and cost savings in maintenance. The economic
benefits of the Project indicate that the overall economic rate of return, as well as that of individual
components, would exceed the opportnity cost of capital. The benefits for the institutional strengthenin
and training components are high but non-qanifiable.
4.37         Economic Evaluation.  The economic analysis of the rehabilitation and periodic
mainrtece of strategic highways was done based on an application of The World Bank's Highway
Design and Mantenan  Standards Model (HDM-f). The economic evaluation of the district road (low-
volume roads) reabilaion component was based on an application of the rural road appraisal



- 22 -
methodology developed by the Bank, which estimates agricultural producer surplus (See details in Annex
11).
4.38         The economic evaluation of the 300 kIn of rehabilitation of strategic hinhwavs was
prepared by international consultants using The World Bank's Highway Design and Maintenance
Standards Model (HDM-Ill) and the recently developed HDM Manager calibrated for Nepal's conditions.
The Economic Rate of Return (ERR) for this component is 19.4% which is substantially higher than the
opportunity cost of capital.
4.39         In the case of the 130 km of rehabilitation of strategic feeder roads, the economic
evaluation was prepared during appraisal by local and expatriate consultants based on an application of
the HDM-U and the HDM Manager with partial data from field condition surveys. Even though the
ERR for this component (10.4%) is slightly higher than the opportunity cost of capital, final feasibility
analyses and envirormental screenig would be requested from the consultants for each road included in
this component, to be reviewed as part of the annual reviews by IDA during the Project.
4.40         The economic evaluation of the 650 km of periodic maintenance of strategic highways
and feeder roads was prepared by local and expatriate consultants based on an application of HDM-r
and the HDM Manager, with field data surveys and using model calibrated for Nepal's conditions. The
road sections proposed for this component are part of the strategic highway network, and were selected
based on a matrix analysis that priorifized roads according to average annual daily traffic (AADT),
roughness (IRD) and surface distress index (SDI). The resulting ERR for this Project component is 29%
which is substantially higher than the opportunity cost of capital. The economic feasibility of the second,
third and fourth year programs would be updated annually to incorporate revised costs and condition
surveys. The updated feasibility analyses and environmental screening for each annual program will be
reviewed anmnally with IDA.
4.41         In the case of the 140 kIn of district road rehabilitation, a group of local consultants
prioritized the network of district roads in four districts with the help of local organizations. After, 100
kn of priority district roads were selected for further analysis. The economic evaluation of these 100
kcm, based on the producer surplus appraisal methodology which has been developed by the Bank,
indicates that the ERR for this component is 36%. The inclusion of the rehabilitation/upgrading of the
Galyang-Chapakot road would require detailed feasibility analysis and environmntal screening for IDA's
review before proceeding with design and preparation of bidding documents.
4.42         The overall ERR for the Project is 21.4%. The sensitivity analysis indicates that the
Project will remain viable if base costs increase 10% and benefits dropped by the same proportion.
F. Iroilect Ris
4.43         At the policy level, the main risks are that the Government will bow to political pressures
in developing it's Priority Investment Plan (PIP) and the related three year rolling sectoral funding
programs. To counter this possibility, the PIP must be satisfactory to IDA (and to the other Donors in
the sector), with annIal agreement on the updated rolling investment/maintenance programs and
maienance allocations. Additionally, the Government must establish a mechanism for funding periodic
emergency and routine maintenance of the road network.
4.44         The main risks for the infrastructural aspects of the project are delays in implementation
and a failure to implement optimal comprehensive maintnce programs. To ensure a prompt start up
of the project, supervisory consultants will be appointed in May 1994, (as will key advisors and
counterpart staff), for road maintenance and rehabilitadon, implementaion of the sector environmental
action plan and for the mechanics and operators training programs. To mitigate against risks of
implementing a sub-opimal maintenance program, the supervising consultants will work closely with



- 23 -
DOR's staff in preparing, tendering and implementing the anmnal work programs. Relevant specialists
will also be available under the institutional development component to provide assistance, particularly
on strategic aspects of the rolling programs. By relating the project to the Government's PIP and annual
development plans with particular emphasis upon maintenance and rehabilitation, the scope of project
works has the flexibility to respond to the implementation experience gained during the early years of the
project. Through the process and planned periodic reviews, alternative approaches can be adopted if
appropriate.
4.45         On the institutional aspects of the project, the main risks are the Government's reluctane
to give DOR the autonomy to reorganize it's operations to focus only on trunk roads, overdependence
upon the consultants' providing technical assistance and a lack of accountability and motivation on the
part of DOR staff. To promote the development of local capabilities, DOR staff will be integral members
of the various technical assistance teams and one of the first task of consultants and advisors will be to
prepare detailed plans for the training of counterpart staff and the gradual transfer of responsibilites from
the consultant to the counterpart staff. Various safeguards, including criteria for counterpart selection,
target handover dates for key responsibilities, operational audits and planned periodic reviews have been
incorporated into the institutional developmen programs in an effort to monitor overall implementaton.
Coinued close cooperation among donors in the sector and carefil monitoring by IDA through project
supervision and an indepth mid-term project review with HMG/DOR in October 1995 will firther help
to minimize the risks.

5.20         Rehabilitation.  The procurement for rehabilitation works includes two phases. The

for emergency rehabilitation works including slope stabilization and base course improvement to be

completed in 2 dry seasons (details in Annex 4, timetable in Annex 10). The phase 2 contracts include:
1 contract for asphalt overlay, 1 contract for surface dressing, I contract for surface dressing and bridge
reconstruction, and 2 contracts for road rehabilitation to gravel standard. All ICB and LCB works will
be bid on the slice and package basis, with the contractor bidding for individual/combined contracts up
to their pre-qualified limit of award.

would consist of 4 contracts for surface dressing and 1 contract for gravelling (11 km of Tansen-Ridi road
