civil works financed by IDA would be as follows: (a) the model road contracts will be for training of pre-
selected contractors under force account; (b) the trial contracts will be procured under limiited local
conmetitive bidding, open only to contractors who are being trained under (a) above; and (c) other road
works wil be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of limited competifive bidding, open only
to contractors who successfully completed the training under (a) and (b) above. All procurement
activities wIll be coordinated with DOR. Procurement of goods financed by IDA will be according to
IDA Guidelines.
5.23         Equipment and Vehicles. Contracts for goods valued (a) US$200,000 and more will be
procured following ICB; (b) US$50,000 - 200,000, up to an aggregate value of US$700,000 following
International shopping (at least three priced quotations); and (c) less than US$50,000 up to an aggregate
value of US$200,000, following Local shopping (at least three priced quotations). Procurement
documentation for all transactions for imen under IDA financing in excess of US$50,000 equivalent
will be subject to prior review by IDA.
5.24         Constalts- Consultants would be selected following Bank Guidelines. All consulting
services contracts above US$100,000 will be subject to prior review by IDA. In addition, prior review
by IDA will be required for (a) Terms of Reference; (b) conts for assigments of a critical nature
(enviromental, safety or public health consideration); and (c) contractual amendments raising the total
contract value above US$100,000.
F. Disbusmenet Account and Audit
5.25         Within the 65% cost sharing, net of taxes and duties, the IDA would disburse for: (a)
civil works, at the rate of 90% for rehabilitation and periodic maintenance expenditures, and 100% for
the training contrac of the pilot labor-based component; (b) equipment, at the rate of 100% of foreign
expenditures and 100% of local expenditures (excluding taxes) of ex-factory prices and lower percentage
if procured locally; and (c) consulting services and training at the rate of 100% of foreign and local
expenditures.
5.26         Statements of expenditures (SOE) will be allowed for expenditmes below the threshold
of US$ 250,000 for civil works and US$ 50,000 for equipment. All other contract expenditures will be
fully documented.



- 29 -
5.27         The Government would establish a Special Account (SA) in US dollars, with an initial
deposit of US$ 2.5 million, to cover local and foreign currency expenditures on Project components. The
IDA would replenish the SA for the amount of withdrawals on account of eligible expenditures at the
request of the Borrower. Agreement during negotiations was reached on the project accounts, financial
statements, SA and Statement of Expenditures, and on providing audit accounts for the Project no later
than twelve months after the end of each fiscal year.
G. Monitorin and Supeon Plan
5.28         Annual Review and Project Plan of Action. In November and April of each year during
Project implementation, IDA, SDC, ODA, UNDP and the Goverment would conduct a formal review
of the progress made in reaching the objectives of the policy initiadves and institutional development
components, and with implementation of the rehabilitation and maintenance programs. The reviews
would provide an opportunity to discuss project issues, including: compliance with the Credit Agreement
covenants; institutional strengthening including improvements in sectoral planning, quality of field
implementation advisors, execution of the training components, implementation of the long tenn
assistance to management (cyclic maintenance, bridges, plant management, geo-environment and road
safety), and implementation of studies; advances regarding the main policy initiatives including
establishment and operation of the mechanism to fund road maitenance, increasing funding for road
maintenance, prioritization of sector expenditures, implementation of sector policies and actions; quality
of subproject implementation and performance of DOR at the national, regional and divisional levels,
including an assessment of the main issues affecting the perfomance of consultants and contractors during
implementaion of the rehabilitation and maintenance programs; and enviromnental analyses. The
discussions and analyses carried out during these reviews would lead to the formulation of adjustments
and, if necessary, remedial actions satisfactory to IDA. The dming and scope of the annual review was
agreed during negotiations.
5.29         During the reviews in Nova.imber of each year, IDA would review the proposed annual
programs for the following fiscal year for each Project component. After updated feasibility studies,
envirownental screening and final programs are prepared for each year, the reviews in April of each year
would review the detailed program, feasibility reports, and the procurement plan. During the reviews
in April of each year IDA would also review, in coordination with SDC, ODA and UNDP, the three year
rolling plan, road sector budget, and progress with the priority investment plan. A mid-term review
would be conducted on or before October 1995.
5.30         Beggring. During negotiations, it was confirmed that: (a) the Project Coordinator in the
Foreign Cooperation Section would be responsible for the preparation of bi-annual reports (by July 15
and January 15) on the progress of the institutional strengthening, policy initiatives and rehabilitation
programs, and quarterly reports on the status of consultant contracts and rehabilitation civil works
contracts; (b) the Project Coordinator in the Maintea  Section would be responsible for the preparation
of annual periodic maintenance programs, amnual proement plans for periodic maintenance, monitoring
and evaluation of the periodic mintenance program at the national level, summarizing quarterly reports
from the Regional Directors for the civil works and procume  contracts under the periodic mantenance
component, monitoring actions to ensure timely preparaton and review by MOWT and IDA of
procurement documentation for periodic maintce civil works and equipment, terms of reference,
letters of invitation, short-lists and draft contas for consulting services, and quarterly progress reports;
and (c) the Project Manager of the DRSDU would be responsible for bi-annual reports on the training
component of the pilot labor-based component and procurement plans for civil works, equipment and
rural transport, quarterly reports for the each subproject under the component, and monitoring and
evaluation of the technical assistance. The reports would discuss compliance with covenants of the Credit
Agreement and actions taken to ensure compliance, including adjustments and remedial actions. DOR
staff would participate in IDA's supervision missions.

are shown in Annex 15. It is expected that at least three supervision missions per year staffed by a
highway engieer and a transport economist will be reqwired during the first three years of project
execution.  In addition, specialists in environmental engineerng, trnsport planning, training, and
procurement may accompany the supervisionniissions as required. Supervision missions will be required
to carry out visits to the regions and divisions involved in the Project to review implementation of the
civil works. The supervision missions would supervise and monitor progress against the action plan and
timetable for the Project (Annex 10). A detailed mid-term review of each Project component will be
carried out before October 1995. The bi-annual reviews by IDA would be carried out during November
and April of each year to cover the issues described above, in coordination with SDC, ODA and UNDP.
During a third supervision mission in each year of the Project, to be scheduled with the Government, a
thematic review would be carried out of a major issue to be resolved. The periodic reviews will cover
agreed actions, target dates, and execution targets included in the maintenance programs agreed at
appraisal, and the status of compliance with all covenants of the Credit Agreement. Specifically, the
reviews would emphasize: (a) the Borrower's commitment to provide counterpart funds and increased,
timely funds for maintenance; and (b) DOR's performance with implementation of the policy and
institutional component of the Project.

NEPAL
ROAID MANTINANCE AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
The Conction-Maintenance Tridde-
In Nepal, it has been common for the road mantace budget to be 'raided' to finance the consruction
of new roads. This has happened both at the budget allocation stage in the MOF and also by the
diversion of allocated fwxds locally as a result of political pressure. This trade-off between constuction
and maintenance, and the implication for the road network in Nepal, may be illustrated using a simple
example.
Consider the road network of Nepal which, in round figures, comprises:
- 3,000 km of paved roads; and
- 5,000 kmn of unpaved roads.
If these roads were in good condition, the anmual mantce budget would need to be:
Paved roads at NR 1.56 lakh per km    = NR 46.8 crore
Unpaved roads at NR 1.02 lakhs per n = NR 51.0 crore
NR 97.8 crore
If, instead of reserving this budget for road maintenance, part of it were diverted to new constructon in
order to extend the road network by just one per cent each year, the following results would emerge:
Paved               Unpaved
Annual incse in length                      30 kn                 S0 kn
Annual new construction cost              30.0 crore           25.0 crore
- at NR 1.0 crora paved
- at NR 0.5 crorekan unpaved
Balance of budget remaiig for road        16.8 crore           26.0 crore
Length of road that could be maintined for  1 0077 km         2 549 kn
this residual budget--_
If this construction-maintenance policy were to coninue for ten years, 800 km of new roads would have
been built. However, a lack of maitenance during this period would have rendered 4,374 kn of road



-3 4-
Page 2 of 2
unserviceable because only 3,626 km of road could be maintined correcdy with the residual budget.
The restoration bill for these 4,734 km of road at the end of ten years can be determind from the
following.
______________________               _  [   Paved                  Unpaved
Length requiring rehabltation            1 923 kmn                2451 km
Assumed average restoration cost per     56.25 laklh              5.75 lakh
kilometer (NRs)                             ____ ______
Tot restoration cost (NRs)               1 081 crore               141 crore
Thus, over a ten year period, the road network would suffer a net reduction of 3,574 Ia: a gain of 800
kn of new roads, as against a loss of 4,374 km of existng roads through lack of maintenance. The total
cost of restoration would be NRs 1,222 crore ($284 million), which is remarkbly similar to the current
estimate of the restoration bacikog in Nepal.
This analysis assumes that the funds available for mantenance would be concated on a restricted
length of 3,626 km. If the funds were, instead, spead across the entire network, the loss of serviceable
roads is likely to be even greater. The current policy of construction and ta   funding ifi Nepal
may be considered against this background.

NEPAL
ROAD MAINTENANCE AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
INSTITUTIONAL STRENGTHENING FINANCED BY IDA UNDER THE RMRP
1.    The institutional strengthening and training component of the Road Maintenance and
Rehabilitation Project has been designed to support the Project's objectives. Within the four years of the
Project the Component will have strengthened the Departnt of Roads' maintenance capabilities,
implemented a progran of institutional strengthening and strengthened the capacity of the local contract
industry.
2.    Several training programs are proposed to be financed under the Project. These are divided into
the programs required for the immediate needs of the Project and for the development needs of the
Department.
3.    Programs required for the immediate needs of the Project will provide: (a) field implementation
advisers for the DOR and contactors on periodic maintenance activities; (b) training for local contractors
in contact biddig procedures and operations for periodic maintenance; and (c) technical assistance and
limited training for contractors on road rehabilitation contracts.
4.    Programs designed to meet the development needs of the Deparanent to be financed mainly by
IDA, SDC, ODA and UNDP include: (a) management and technical training for the Department's staff
(SDC and ODA); (b) visits to other road agencies; (c) training for District Development Committees and
small contractors in labour-based rehabilitation technology for local roads in support of the policy of
increasing the role of Districts in infrastructure maintenance (UNDP); (d) strengthening support will be
given to the Department's Mechanics and Operators Training Center to increase the range and
effectiveness of the training given (IDA); (e) support for Strengthened Maintenance Divisions (SDC) to
undertake network planning and coordinate work on the strategic network; (f) training in periodic
miaintenance management, contracts and work methods for the Departnent's staff, consultants and
contractors (ODA); (g) establishing a bio-engineering capacity, a bridge management system (including
special requirements for the Karnali Bridge) and a plant management system in the Department (IDA and
ODA); (h) establishing a road safety program in Nepal (ODA).
5.    Wherever possible existing local resources should be used, augmented where necessary by
expatriate inputs.
6.    Central to this Project component is the establishment within the DOR of a Road Sector Skills
Manager, in the Road Sector Skil Development Unit, reporting to the Director General. The Road
Sector Skdlls Manager will provide advice and assistance to the Department as well as to other
components of the Project. The Manager will provide coordination and limited management for the
institutional strengthening and training components, especially ensuring the training meets professional
standards.
7.    The Road Sector Skills Manager will be resourced to undertake actions approved by a Steering
Committee representing the Ministry of Works and Transport, the Ministry of Finance, the National
Planning Committee, funding agencies and the Director General of Roads.



- 36 -
Annex 2
Page 2 of 27
8.    It is proposed that the Road Sector Skills Manager be assisted by a training adviser.
9.    Linkages will be established with existing training providers to provide certificated training
wherever possible. These providers will be asked to administer training programs specific to the needs
of the Departnent. These programs will be part of a process of systematic staff development that will
continue after the Project.
10.   The following actions are recommended to implement the IDA funded components of the
institutional strengthening and training component of the RMRP: (a) IDA and other funding agencies to
fonralize a Coordination Committee among donors in the sector as soon as possible; (b) IDA, and other
funding agencies, and the Inter-Ministerial Committee on Road Maintenance, to form an Institutional
Development Steering Committee as soon as possible; (c) DOR to invite proposals from consulting firms
for the team of Field Implementation Advisers; (d) DOR to explore with the supervision consultants of
the phase I rehabilitation contracts the inclusion of training for contractors on the rehabilitation contracts;
(e) DOR to engage the Filed Implementation Advisors according to Terms of Reference (Appendix)
before May 15, 1994.
OBJECTIVES
i1.    The institutional strengthening and training component of the RMRP has two objectives: (a)
immediately to augment and expand the existing capacity of the DOR, contractors and consultants to meet
the needs of the physical investment components of the RMRP; and (b) over several years to develop the
capacity of the DOR to manage the road network.
12.   An underlying objective is to foster an awareness of the need for change. This should evolve
over several years as a result of the strengthening and training efforts in the RMRP.
STRATEGY
50.   The recommended strategy for Institutional Development agreed during appraisal and confirmed
during negotiations is to: (a) strethen the DOR initially while studying the possibility for future
reform; (b) developing field implementation capabilities; (c) focus the DOR on the main road network
and decentralize district roads; (d) remove constraints to staff development.
13.   Initial strengthening should be completed within the period of the RMRP. The PAP Workshop
of 1993 has recommended the reform study with a view to explore the advantages of an independent
roads authority.
14.   The strategy recommended for staff training and development is similar to that for Institutional
Development: (a) strengthen the DOR initally; (b) coordinate the training with the institutional
strengthening process, recognizing the interdependence of the two processes.

IMMEDIATE INTERVENTONS
A. CONTRACTOR TRAINING FOR PERIODIC MAINTENANCE
15.   Review. HMGIN has a policy to stimulate the domestic road contracting industry. As part of this
policy the DOR intends to enhance the ability of contractors to bid for local and international contracts
in periodic maintenance of paved and gravel roads. This will be done under the RMRP.
16.   This will build on work already begun by SDC in Nepal when a recent course on contract
procedures was strongly supported by over 80 contactors and on work by IDA in the Flood
Rehilitation Program. The SDC will continue this course (with specific reference to periodic
maintenance for DOR contracts) under the RMRP. The IDA will support training in field operations and
techniques to ensure the backlog of rehabilitation and resurfacing work can be reduced.
17.   Objective. The objective for this component will be to support the development of a local
contractor industry by:
a. Training contractors to understand contract documents and prepare bids.
b.  Training contractors in field operations.
18.   The training objective will be achieved by training contractors wanting to bid for periodic
maintenance contracts under the RMRP, by training successful bidders in field operations on site and by
supporting the Contractors' Association of Nepal.
19.   Trainina process. The training will be given in three stages:
a. Training as part of the ODA funded contracts on contract documentation and bidding procedures.
Where appropriate this training will be provided in the Nepalese language;
b. Assistance to contractors as they prepare their bids (except for pricing).
c.  Training of managers and supervisors on the work site from mobilization through to making the
final claim, as part of the ODA financed training contracts and through the input of the Field
Implementation Advisers (TOR enclosed attached).

D. SUPPORT FOR THE MECHANICS AND OPERATORS TRAINING CENTER
39.   The MOTC was established with a World Bank loan in 1983 and further developed by the ODA
in 1986-87. The MOTC has tained 28 (out of 382) operators, 75 (of 224) helpers and 55 (of 104) junior
mechanics. It has not trained any electricians or machinists. This means that a total of 158 people (of a
possible 1012) have been trained.
40.   Survisos and senior mehanics have not been trained. It is reported that, as a result, they have
discouraged the use of new skills by juniors on return to work. They are not able to see the value in the
new methods.
41.   Many mechanics have poor literacy and numeracy skills. This is a significant constraint to
competency becase training and work reuires meaurments and calcuations as well as reference to
written materia.
42.   When staff leave their work site for training at the MOTC they lose their site subsistence
allowance. Some staff have retuned from training at the MOTC to find their places have been taken by
other recruits.
43.   The deifct of taining has been constrained by:

b.  the wide range of equipment, makes and models used in DOR;
c.  the lack of appropriate tools and equiment in some workshops.
d. low literacy and numeracy skills.
44.   Training itself has been constrined by:
a. insufficient operating fiuds;
b.  the lack of machine tools and laboratory equipment for trning;
c.  onbl one instuctor and no dedicated equipment for operator raining;
d.  the fact that most proposed training progras have not been designed yet;
e. reluctance of staff to leave their work place.

57.   The Foreman Mechanic will also work with DOR mechanical staff where necessary to ensure
that all equipmt used for the RMRP is competently serviced and maintaned.
58.   The Mechanical Engineer will be engaged for a total of 21 months in three periods: (a) starting
in July 15, 1994 for 15 months, (b) starting in October 1996 for three months, and (c) starting in January
1998 for three months. The Foreman Mechanic expatriate will be hired for a total of 30 months in two
periods: (a) starting in July 15, 1994 for 24 months, (b) starting in january 1998 for 6 months. Subject
to the result of a needs assessment by the Mechanical Engineer a local Foreman Mechanic or Operator
trainer will be engaged full-time until the completion of the project.

for
FEID IDLEENTATION ADVISORS
OBJECTIVE
1.    The first objective of the institutional strengthemng and tranig component of the RMRP is
immediately to augment and expand the existing capacity of DOR's regional offices and divisions,
contractors and consultants to meet the needs of the physical investnent components of the RMRP.
2.    These Terms of Reference describe the interventions proposed to meet this objective.
FM"D I      AMPLEETATION ADVISERS FOR PERIODIC MAINTENANCE ACTIVITIES
3.    The bacldog periodic maitence program under RMRP. In the first year it will involve 114
kms in three regions, increasing to 160 kms in three regions in the second year, 175 kms in four regions
in the third year and 160 kns in four regions in the fourth year. This requires technical assistance to be
provided to the Maitna    Section of DOR and Divisional Engineers, and it is proposed to be funded
by IDA.
4.    The intention is to provide assistance and training to management staff of the DOR and on the
job traing to local contractors and associated consultants.
5.     The Field Implementation Advisers (17A) will develop systems and procedures for maintenance
that reflect current DOR requirements.
6. ManLgeVunt. Over the period of the RMRP there will be one expatriate consultant engaged as road
maintenance engineer (increased input to be discussed during mid-term review) and two local consultants
engaged as assistant road maintenance engnees.
7.     They will form a team under the management of one consultant to:
a.  Assist the management of DOR in monitoring the backlog periodic maintenance program under
the RMRP.
b.  Develop management, quality and finacial control systems for contract implementation and
regularly assess progress and performance of consultants and contractors.
c.  Provide on the job training to contractors and DOR staff and consultants involved in
implementation of the backlog periodic maintenance program.
d.  They will assist the DDW (Maintenance), Regional Directors and Divisional Engineers prepare
quarterly reports assessing contractual matters, evaluating proposals from contractors and
consultants and developing solutions to implementation problems.



- 46 -
Page 12 of 27
8.    Subject to review at the time it is envisaged that one road maintenance engineer will be full-time
for the project duration, with additional input if required to join the team in 1995-96 and 1996-97 as the
work load increases. One assistant engineer will be full-time from the beginning of the 1994-95 year until
the completion of the project, the second full-time for 1996-97 and 1997-98.
9.     Ouality control. There will be up to two local consultants and up to two staff seconded from
DOR engaged as quality control advisers in the Field Implementation Advisors staff to advise DOR. They
will ensure the quality control system is being properly followed by laboratory staff. It is expected that
two advisers will be engaged for a total of 21 months in the year 1994-95, three advisers full-time in
1995-96 and four full-time in both 1996-97 and 1997-98.
10.   One materials engineer will be engaged for short-term visits in each of the first three years to
review the quality conrol systems in place, determine appropriate testing procedures and analyze the
tring needs of consultants and DOR staff. This person will ensure the work of the quality control
advisers meets the requirements of the quality control system. About five months input is envisaged for
the year 1994-95 and two months in each of the following two years.
11.   Train     in opeLrions. An experienced field supervisor will be engaged to work with the
conactors on the work site.
12.   The objective of this component will be to train contractors' supervisory staff in mobilization,
resurfacing techniques and site control.
13.   The training will be matched to the contract specifications on "training contcs". These will
be similar in all respects to other contracts with specific allowance in the conditions for the training input.
14.   The training will include: mobilizing resources; managing resources during the contact;
controlling quality and progress to meet the contract specifications and programs; completing the conract;
undertaking the maintenance period; road resurfacing techniques for paved roads; road resurfacing
techniques for gravel roads; storing materials and equipment; site management and supervision; training
workers; improving productivity.
15.   The taining will be practical, on the job and direcdy related to the on-going work required to
meet the contract conditions and specifications.
16.   The field operations supervisor will make short term inputs during the constuction season. These
will be for about 5 months in 1994-95, 4 months in 1995-96, 3 months in 1996-97 and 2 months in 1997-
98.
17.   Consultancy packge. The scope of work described for the FIA is suitable for a package for
a local consuldng group, strengthened by expatriate advice where needed. The advantages of such a
package include:
a. It would help build local consultant capacity.
b. It would provide a more uniform input (than if consultants were engaged individually).
c.  It would be more easily managed by the DOR (than individual ins).
d. It would assist DOR's management ensure quality, progress and low cost.

program of periodic, recurent and routine maintenance for roads (which can be fully implemented once

BACKGROVND
21.    His Majesty's Government of Nepal (HMG/N) wishes to improve the maintenance of its road
network.
22.   In Nepal maintenance of the nadonal road network is the responsibility of the Department of
Roads (DOR). The network of about 7,330 kn includes 2,958 km of paved roads, the rest having a
gravel or earth surface. The DOR has resources to perform maintenance activities on only one-fourth of
the network.
23.    The DOR is beginning to focus on increasing the capacity for periodic and routine maintenance
of the trunk roads that are in a maiainable condition and to develop network-based planning for
systematic mainenance and rehabilitation. The responsibility for local roads will be given to District
Developmen Committees, municipalities and villages.
24.   HMG/N has received strong support from various funding agencies including the International
Development Association (IDA), the Asian Development Bank (ADB), the Swiss Development Co-
operation (SDC), the Overseas Development Association of UK (ODA) and the United Nations
Development Program (UNDP) who have agreed to focus futumre support on safeguarding past investments
and supporting the development of road management capabilities.
25.    HMG/N has requested IDA to finance a proposed project to undertake a rehabilitation and
maintenance program. One objective of this program is to remove the bacldog of periodic maintenance
work through a series of contracts over a period of four years.

HIS MAJESTY'S GOVERNMENT OF NEPAL
MINISTRY OF WORKS AND TRANSPORT, DEPARTMENT OF ROADS
ROAD MAINTENANCE AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
TERMS OF REFERENCE
FOR
MECHANICAL ENGINEER for the MECHANICS & OPERATORS TRAINING CENTER
BACKGROUND
1. His Majesty's Govermnent of Nepal (HMG/N) wishes to improve the maintenance of its road network.
2. In Nepal maintenance of the national road network is the responsibility of the Department of Roads
(DOR). The network of about 7,330 km includes 2,958km of paved roads, the rest having a gravel or
earth surface. The DOR has resources to perform maitenance activities on only one-fourth of the
network.
3. The DOR is beginning to focus on increasing the capacity for periodic and routine maintenance of the
tunk roads that are in a maintauinable condition and to develop network-based planning for systematic
maintenance and rehabilitation. The responsibility for local roads will be given to District Development
Committees, municipalities and villages.
4. HMG/N has received strong support from various funding agencies including the International
Development Association (IDA), the Asian Development Bank (ADB), the Swiss Development Co-
operation (SDC), the Overseas Development Association of UK (ODA) and the United Nations
Development Program (UNDP) who have agreed to focus future support on safeguarding past investments
and supporting the development of road management capabilities.
5. HMG/N has asked the IDA to finance a proposed Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project
(RMRP) to undertake a rehabilitation and maiteance program which includes a substantial component
relating to the institutional strengthening of the DOR. A key element of this strengthening will be the
expansion and improvement of its Mechanics and Operators Training Center (MOTC) at Lalitpur.
6. It is intended that the MOTC will be  engthened to meet the needs of the DOR for the maintenance
of the road network, including the implementation of the RMRP, and to meet the increasing demand for
skilled staff in the growing equipment and contracting industries in Nepal.
OBJECT WE
7. To strengthen the MOTC to provide the range of traning required by the DOR.
8. To recommend future directions for the MOTC.
9. Develop the capacity of mechanics and operators to service and operate available equipment efficiently.

SCOPE
10.   The consultant's work will include:
a. comment on existing proposals for institutional strengthening of the MOTC, including the
expansion of the facilities, the procurement of new or rehabilitation of any old equipment for
workshop, classroom and on site training, mobile operator training, office equipment and
transport;
b.  study and recotmmend procedures for insttutional stengthening;
c. recommend procedures for optimizing the use of the MOTC facilities, including training
mechanics and operators from outside the DOR and using the facilities for other training;
d.  examine the options and make recommendations on operator skills training;
e.  manage the implementation of strengteing processes including the procurement of any new,
or the rehabilitation of any old, equipment;
f. liaise with the Road Sector Skills Manager in the DOR in all aspects of the strengthening
program;
g.  review the existing training provided at the MOTC;
h. revise, where necessary and design additional training courses for mechanics and operators in
the classroom and on the job site;
i.  train the existing and new instructors to carry out the revised and new trainig courses;
j. work with the Road Sector Skills Manager and odter training institutions to identify providers
of appropriate supervisory training for the MOTC and negotiate with them;
k.  similarly, identify the providers of appropriate literacy and numeraq traiing and negotiate with
them;
1. work with the consultants advising the Strengthened Maintenance Divisions Program in regard

Equipment Divisions;
m.  work with the consultants in order to integrate the on-site training of mechanics and  ators
into the site work program;
n.  review existing procedures for store-keeping in the MOTC and assist with implementation of
changes;

NEPAL
ROAD MAINTENANCE AND REHABILJTATION PROJECT
RehabilitaioE Compogn
The scope of the rehabilitation component mcludes six sections of strategic highways including the
following works: (a) Thankot-Naubise (12 kam) - overlay and minor pavement widening to a 6 m. standard
with 0.5 m paved shoulders, exting fhm side drains and providing lay-byes where practicable, lining
of outfall surface drains,  n      and width restoration at km 21.5, (b) Marsyangdhi-Pokhara (44
Ian) - overlay and minor widening to 5.5. m in full or part, (c) Butwal-Tansen (37 kan) - overlay and
slope stabilization works (latter to be Ihnited to containm, repair and erosion control methods rather
than full miigation of slope hazards), (d) Mugling-Namyanghat (36 km) - overlay which should be
retained for last slice of the proam, (e) Tansen-Syangja (84 kn) - surface dressing to ensure better
performance, and (e) Naubise-Hetauda (107 km) - coimnment of landslides, improvement of cross and
side drainage and river trainig and protection works, base course repairs and surface dressing (to address
the July 1993 floods). This component also includes rebabihtation of 130 km of sategic feeder roads
including resaling of Birguij-Kallya, regravelling of Tansen-Ridi-Tanigas, and Nepalgumj-Bagauda,
sbjoect to detailed fasibility studies and environmet sm   accordn  to the SEA Guidelihes
(preliminary feasibility prepred during Project preparation indicates that low-cost rehabilitation could
be justfied if the existng carriage widh is mained, improvements in side and cross-drainage are
mphasized and  onuon of bridges is undeaken only when stictdy necessary. The preparatory
work for the rhabilitaton   sategic higways has been reviewed with DOR and the selection of the
above mentioned (optimal) tratents has been agreed between IDA and DOR. The final costs were
prepared based on the final eineering design and were confirmed during negotiations.
The rehabilitation component will be implenented in two phases. Pbase 1 includes: (a) 92 km of
asphalt overlay and rehabilitation on 3 strategic highways (i.e., Thankot-Naubise (12 kn), Marsyangdhi-
Pokhara (44 km), and Butwal-Tansen (37 hIn)), (b) 107 kn of emergency rehabilitation of the Naubise-
Hetauda road, and (c) 11 kn of base course and surface dressing of the Chaudar-Gaddachauki road
(which for practical purposes is incuded in the periodic mantenance cost tables as part of the Atada-
Gaddachauki road). Phasc 2 includes: (a) two sections of strategic highways (i.e., Mugling-Narayanghat
(36 km) - asphalt overlay (to be retamined for the last year), and Tansen-Syanaja (84 kn) - a periodic
maintenance surface drssing to ensure better performance); and (b) three strategic feeder roads, subject
to results of envi       screening and final feasibility analysis to be completed during Project
implementation, including: (i) Bigunj-Kalaiya (15 kn) - surface dressing and reconstuction of a timber
bridge, (ii) Ridi-Tamgas (58 kmn) - regravelling, and (iii) Nepalgunj-Bagauda (50 kn) regraling and
bridge constuction. The implementation of phase 1 has sarted with advanced procement actions
expected to include conact award before May 1994, and works to be completed before June 1996. The
implementaion of phase 2 wors will start in June 1995 and is expected to be completed by June 1997.



- 67 -
Annex 4
Page 2 of 3
Status of Prenaration
Feasibility reports were prepared by N.D. Lea Associates for the phase 1 works. During a review
of the report at appraisal IDA confirmed that it addressed comments made on a previous version during
pre-appraisal. The analysis presented in the report follows a generally sound techno-economic
methodology utilizing the HDM-l model, calibrated to Nepalese conditions. The application is based
on mostly original survey data, but also utilizes previous studies to the extent possible, with judicious
modifications where appropriate (for example, in calibrating estimates of vehicle operating costs (VOC)).
The evaluations are being improved with additional technical assessment of viable options on each site
(further assessment of structural design estimates, single vs. double surface treatment, shape correction
needs, etc.), which are to be incorporated in the final phase of the services. As a result, the
recommendations of treatments are basically sound, and were reviewed durig the appraisal mission,
resulting in an upward revision of estimated costs.  During Project preparation site specific
recommendations were incorporated in the bidding documents by the consultants.
The consultants have completed the final engineering design and bidding documents based on
IDA's Sample Bidding Documents. The model SBDs and the list of changes to the SBDs for the phase
I works have been cleared by IDA before negotiations (i.e., with the exception of the emergency works
for the Naubise-Hetauda emergency works which have been cleared following negotiations, and the design
for slope stabilization in the Butwal-Tansen road which would be prepared during Project
inplementation). Given the nature of the slope stabilization recommended for the Butwal-Tansen and
Mugling-Narayanghat roads, it was agreed that engineering design of this sub-conponent would be part
of the construction supervision services and the Terms of Reference were agreed to during negotations.
The additional engineering design for the phase 2 works would be undertaken as part of the supervision
contract as envisioned in the LOI to the consultants selected for phase 1, which includes assistance with
bid evaluation for the phase 1 contacts.
In order to initiate the rehabilitation and overlays during the FY94 dry season, prequalification of
contractors (for the phase 1 works) has been carried out. Tenders are expected to be invited by Februarv
15. 1993, and contcts awarded by May 31. 1994 (after credit effectiveness). This schedule allows only
3.5 months for tendering and bid evaluation.
The Supervision and Desin Technical Assistance as stated in the LOI would include a chief
resident engineer, specialist inputs from a surface dressing and asphalt specialist. In addition, as
discussed above, a geotechnical engineer (visiting specialist for 12 staff moths) would be required for on-
site design and supervision of slope stabilization works under this Project component. The present
consultant services contract would be modified to include assistance with bid evaluation sometime in
February 1994. DOR informed that the consultants have been requested to submit a proposal for
supervision services before credit effectveness, to be evaluated and a recommendation to award
forwarded to IDA before board presentation.
The procue     timetable for this Project co nt requires a series of carefillly planned actions
which are outlined in Amnex 10. In order to start the contracts under the first phase after credit
effectiveness and to at least have the contractors mobilize during before 10194 ('94-'95 dry season), the
following implementation time table would need to be followed:

During negotiations agreement was confirmed that rehabilitation works over US$ 1 million would
be procured under ICB. Contractors will be requested to bid for each single slice and to offer discounts
if awarded more than one slice. It is estimated that the phase 1 works could be completed in 2-3 dry
seasons, i.e. latest by 96/97.
The works to be packaged under LCB should encourage an active participation of national
contractors in the bidding. The success of the strategy will largely depend upon the success of the
Training Activities foreseen under the Project. In order to simplify the commercial sections of the tender
documents for the benefit of the national contractors, the Bidding Documents have been prepared
according to the Bank's SBD for "smaller contracts".

1.  Bdum
Routine, recrrent and periodic maintenance activities are in ent and, in combination,
represent a cost effective meas of retaiing the seiceability of a road and delaying the need for more
expensive rebabilitation measures.  Of these acti es, those comprising routine and recurrent
maitenance of the drainage and carriageway are a prereisite for an economic penodic maitenance
programme.
Routine and recrent maintenc activities should be carried out every year on each road link in
the network. The ann is to orgaise the work in a regular and sysematc manmer such that an adequate
standard of work is achieved and resources are used effectively. In this way a greaer length of road can
be maintained with the available funds.
The technical requiement of routinrecurrent maintenance are low, the problem is one of
management rather than of enginering. The key is to undertake relatively small amouns of work
routinely rather than large amounts at iequent ineals. To achieve this, the Divisional Engineer must
have detailed infonnation on the sections of road to be maintaind (the road inventory), the operating
conditons (affic, climate, constuction materi) and the history and present condition of the pavement.
Feedback on the perormnce levels and sufficiency of the various maitenance activities are also
essential.
The following components of Division leve operations will therefo  need to be addressed
concurently:
* management systems;
*  implementation methods;
* standards of workmansbip.
MAD     M Systems - a magement system for routne/recurnt maintenance activities is basically
any logical process involving work progra     and resomce schedulig. Various approaches are at
present being tried in Nepal on the Eastn Region Road Maitenance Project and the Lamosagu-Jiri
Road Manten  Project supported by ODA and SDC respectively. However, it is extremely difficult
to transfer externally developed project systems direcy into the DOR oganition. Further development
and adaton is usually needed wbich can only be deermid though testmg in the DOR working
enVironment.

2.       _bne   Usibennc DhSmm  SMDs)
The stategy for obtning improvements in routinel recurrent maitn     and the mangent of
emergency maintenance is to introduce, test, develop and consolidate new systems and procedures within
Stugthened Maintea  Divisions (SMDs). The SMDs represent a pilot operaion and will comprise
existing DOR Divisions under the control of the Divisional Engineer. Limited technical assistance and
basic infastructur support will be provided for the SMDs.
The approach will be to review present operations in these divisions and propose changes where
needed rather than attempt to introduce completely new systems. As noted, valuable experience can be
gained from the work already being undertaken by on-going donor funded maitenan  projects and close
liaison will be maitained with them. The information acqired should provide useful guidelines for the
SMDs and base data for comparison. In tum, the results of the SMD exercise will be fed back to the
projects.
The strategy is programmed to start with two Divisions in fiscal year 93194. If these are successful,
the exercise will be gsauy extended to cover the whole of the Strategic Network. It is envisaged that
there would be 3 more SMDs in operation during fisca year 94195, increasing at a rate of about 3 per
year onwards.  However, man        activities can only be effective on roads which are in a
maintable condition. The expansion of the SMDs is therefore closely linked with the rehabilitation
and resealing programmes of the World Bank Road Maitenance Project and other projects for
rehabilitation and new constuction on the Strategic Network.
The DOR has selected Chitwan, and Lalitpur as the initial SMDs. These Divisions are in Central
Region and therefore within reasonable distance of Katmandu to facilitate monitoring, they also
encompass maintainble roads in the Terai, mid-hills and urban environments.

under normal DOR operaing conditions. Support -;lI, however, be needed in the form of limited

If maintenance operations are to be carried out efficiently, wt only is an adequate level of funding
important but, equally, disbursement must be relatively free from constraints. At present, a combination

within MOWTIDOR. The fimd will cover the costs of routine, recurrent, penodic and emergency
maitenance on the strategic network and will be financed by direct road user revenues (tolls) and a
proportion of indirect road user revenues from fuel taes. These revenues would be transferred to the
fimd directly at source and the level of the fimd would be a minimum compatible with supportg the trial
maitce operations in the SMDs.

NEPAL
ROQD MAINTENANCE AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
Pilot Labor Based District Road Rehabilitation Component
This project component would support a Pilot District Road Rehabilitation and Maintenance Fund
to provide technical and financial support to five (5) District Development Committees (DDCs) in
Western region in order to promote, or a pilot basis, low-cost, labor-based appropriate technology in road
rehabilitation. The districts selected for the pilot project, based on critena designed to make the new
labor-based program successful, include Nawalparasi, Rupandehi, Kapilbastu, Gulmi and Syangia.
The Government supports the devolution of responsibilities for the district and village road
networs to local organizations. However, while DOR plans to transfer the responsibility for local access
roads to the DDCs and VDCs, it is important to have a national focal point to help plan for rural roads,
to tackle common problems, to ensure national priorities and planning, and to deal with international
fimding agencies for rural road projects. For the pilot program, a District Road Services Development
Unit (I)RSDU) attached to the Secretary in the Miistry of Local Development will fill this vacuum.
Objectives
Tis project component will support low-cost, labor-based appropriate technology in road
rehabiliaion and maintenance by small contractors in four pilot districts in the Western region of Nepal
around Butwal. The project supports three main Government policies: (a) improvement of rural access
by rehabilitating and maiaining district level roads; (b) allevation of povert by the creation of gainful
employment; and, (c) decentralization by strengthening the District level local govetnment.
The pilot project's immediate objectives are to: (a) strengthen the capacity of the DDCs to: develop
planning capabilities, monitor procuement of civil works under contract, supervision with local
consultants, and to oversee a program of maintenance and financing of the district transport retwork; (b)
train small local contractors, DDC staff and DoR staff in the execution and supervision of the
rehabilitation and matenance of transport infiastucture using labor-based techniques (labor-based are
those techniques that use the  ptmm mix of labor and equipment in order to achieve the lowest cost);
(c) rehabilitate and maitain priority links of the District transport network using small local labor-based
contractors; (d) prepare a follow-p project that will replicate the approach in other districts and that will
apply labor-based methods in other fields of activity in the Districts.
The roads selected for the pilot project, based on consultation with DDCs, pre-screening based on
crteria of suitability for the program, and final feasibility analyses are the following: (a) Manigram-
Devdaha (7.8 kn), (b) Bbaiawa-Parasi (17.8 kn), (c) Kxishannagar-Chotagawanpur (19 km), (d)
Bardaghat-Pratpur (14.7 kn), (e) Maheshpur-Parasi (12.4 km), MRM (Gorusinghe)-Patharkot (11 kIn),
(f) Lumbini-Koi-Taulihawa (22 kn) (regravel followed immediately with base course and SBST as part
of the periodic mance progrm subject to final feasibility and environmental screening according
to the SEA Guidelines), (g) Babdurganj-Tahawa (9 km), and (h) Naudanda-Karkineta (6 kn). In
addition, the Galang-Chapakot road (Syangja District) has been proposed and would be considered for

fesibilitv and environmental screening accordinSg to the SEA Guidelines.

MonitorinulEvaluation
It is important to design a Monitoring/Evaluation system to assess the program and its socio-

by the local consultants who did initial economic and other studies for preparing this component, as this
wfill help in contimity and better undersadg of the socio-economic data.
mra on the environment
Most of Nepal is located in the mountain chains of the Himalayas, these relatively young mountain
chains are very vulnerable to weathering, and erosion and landslides are present everywhere. The erosion
threatens irrigation schemes and roads. Road construction disturbs the precarious equilibrium of the
mounin slopes and erosion control is therefore an absolute necessity. The measures required for erosion
control are very labor intensive: tree planting, gabions, dry stone walls, small drainage structures in
masonry, etc. Labor-based construction methods are therefore well suited for road construction in the
hill districts of Nepal. Some projects have already tested environment-friendly construction techniques
like bio-engneeing on the Palpa project (funded by Helvetas) and green roads (GTZ). The project should
elaborate on these techniques and further the generalization of the use of these techniques. This will have
an exemplary effect and thereby may positively influence other projects in Nepal
pr         arem
The DRSDU will select the contractors for training from the Pilot Districts, based on advertisement
and objective screening criteria which will emphasize good performance in previous contracts. The Pilot
Project would aim at training Class B, C and D contractors in labor-based appropriate technologies
sulemented with small equipment. Since many 'B' class contractors own heavy equipment, not more
than 1/3 of the selected conractors will be from B" category, to avoid conflict of interest with the new
technology.

million, NRs 3 million, NRs 1 million); and
(c) after the trial period and for the rest of the pilot project works, competition will be limited

consider (i) increasing the above thresholds which wert fixed 12 years ago, by at least

It is expected that at the end of the project the districts will be able to mnage their transport
infrastructure. They will have leamed to use local consultants and small labor-based contractors. The
local consultants and small labor-based coators will have been trained by the project to operate as
small businesses applying labor-based techniques. The small contractors will have been able to acquire
the small equipment they need. Based on the experience in other countries, the unit costs of the works
under this method will be less than under the capital-intensive method. The small labor-based contractors
will thus be able to compete with equipment-based contractors and win contracts in a competitive market
after the project period.

based techniques in other fields of application. Funding for the follow-up study are provided under the

ROAD MAINTENANCE AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
PORITY INVESTMENT PLAN (ROAD MASTER PLAN)
FOR THE STRATEGIC ROAD NETWORK AND RURAL TRANSPORT
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR CONSULTING SERVICES
I. OBECTIES
1.   The main objectives of the consulting services are:
(a)  to define the basic grid on which the strategic road network and the rural sport network
should develop, their integration, and the optimal phasing of investments and maintenance
over a 10-year period, taking into account environmental, social and economical
considerations;
(b) to determnme the optimal balance between investments and maintenance;
(c)  to determine the optimal balance among:
(i) constuction of new roads of the strategic road network (national highways and
feeder roads and brdges);
(ii) improvement, rehabilitation and maintenance of the existing strategic road network;
(iii) development of the rural transport network (district roads, trails and suspension
bridges); and
(iv) improvement, rehabilitation and maintenance of the existing rural transport network;
(d)  to determine an appropriate level of expenditures on investment and maintenance given local
resources and absorptive capacity constraints (financial, technical, and human resource
related) and the probable level of foreign assistance in the sub-sector in the next few years:
(e)  to assist in preparing a Priority Investment Plan and review process under the policy
objectives set by the Eighth Five Year Plan (EFYP) identifying potential programs and
projects for the stegpc network and the rural transport network for financing by the major
donors active in the sector; and
(f)  to provide appropriate training to NPC's Transport Unit and MOWTIDOR and other local
staff in the Ministry of Local Development (MLD) in modern road planming and analytical
techniques.



- 84 -
Annex 7
Page 2 of 8
II. SCOPE OF WORK
2.   The Consultants shall carry out all engineering and economic stud."s, including topographical
surveys and pavement strength tests, needed to attain the objectives stated above. The analyses would
have two major components: (a) strategic road network, and (b) rural transport network. Regarding the
strategic road network, sectoral investments on development and upgrading wIll be conducted and overall
maintenance strategies would be developed based on the World Bank's Highway Design and Maintenance
Model (HDM  l) which has been recently calibrated for Nepal's conditions developing the regions in a
balanced manner. Regarding the rural transport network, an integrated rural transport strategy would be
developed for development and maintenance of district and rural roads, trails and suspension bridges and
appropriate means of transport, based on the final reports of the District Network Analysis. Following
is a more detailed description of the main tasks to be undertaken by the Consultants:
Review of Technical Studies and Detailed Work Progam
3. The Consultants wiDl review all relevant studies carried out in the subsector in the past and extract
any information that may be used by the present study. Based on this information and their initial
findings, the consultants will update and revise the original work plan submitted with their proposal and
provide a detailed schedule of work including the timing of key events and present it as part of the
Incedtion Report.
Traffic Forecasts and Network Analvsis
4.   Traffic Sudies.   Regarding the strategic road network, the Consultants will review the existing
affic data collected and analyzed by MRCU, N.D. Lea International (for the Road Maintenance Project)
and other consulants, prepare and carry out a program of additional data collection, and assess present
road traffic demand. The program will include, to the extent required, additional traffic surveys
(classified counts and axle load surveys) and origin/destinaton surveys for passenger and goods traffic.
Regarding the rural transport network, the Consultants will review the traffic data available, district road
network analysis, and ex-ante and ex-post feasibility studies of district and rural roads, trails and
suspension bridges to assess parameters of integrated rural transport demand.
5. Traffic Forecasts. The Consultants will prepare traffic volume and mix forecasts for highways
and feeder roads. The choice of appropriate foreating approach will be preceded and determined by
an assessment and analysis of: (i) overall economic development prospects and the resulting total transport
demand, (ii) the likely future modal distnrbution of traffic between strategic roads, rural transport network
and other modes of tranWort, and (iii) the demand to be met by the strategic road network and the rural
tLanwsport network in Nepal. Different scenanos could be developed and evaluated as appropriate.
6. Wherever predominantly stable growth conditions and only gradual changes in socio-economic
conditions are expected to prevail, the Consultants will extrapolate from past trends, making adjustments
that may be necessary to account for changes that are likely to modify these trends. Where major and
rapid changes in socio-economic conditions are expected, the consultants will prepare traffic forecasts in
three stages: (i) estimation of volume and location of future tourism, agricultural, industrial and mining
input and consumption, including imports and exports; analogous estimates of population and personal
income growth and of the inwcome elasticity of demand for transport will be necessary; (ii) translation of
population ad outpuconsumption forecasts into traffic - both by volume and by origin and destination



- 85 -
Annex 7
Page 3 of 8
and; (iii) distribution of traffic to the various transport modes, taking into account both perceived and
total distribution costs and any major planned transport projects (such as the tunnel between Kathmandu
and Hetauda, and the Banepa-Sindhuli road). The resulting road transport growth rates will be applied
to the base year traffic estimates to produce road traffic forecasts for the strategic road network and
parameters to estimate demand for the rural transport network.
7.   The Consultants should consider the almost uni-modal set-up of Nepal's transport system. Use of
a traffic assignment and forecasting model shall be supported by the rationale for the Consultant's choice
and present it as part of their proposal.
8.   On the basis of the traffic forecasts and taking into account the limited existing network, the
consultants will assess whether the segments additional to the existing grid of roads (including upgrading
of existing roads) are necessary to meet future traffic demand and sustainable taking into account
environmental considerations. Regarding the strategic road network, the Consultnts would prepare a
simple model to forecast traffic based on the above analyses. Regarding the rural transport network, the
Consultants should develop through a consultative process with District Development Committees and
local organizations (e.g., users groups) a revised assessment of demand for rural transport at the district
level based on the District Road network Analysis. The demand for roads would be, e.g., a function of
productive potential, social service delivery needs, accessibility, aggregate transport cost functions and
resource mobilization capacities (local and central level).
Technical Data Collection
9.   Strategic Network. The consultants will review and update the following techical data in the
format reqred by the HDM-M model.
(a)   Vehicle Opaing Costs. The Consultants will review and update the calibrated VOC
model prepared for the Road Mainance Project.
(b)  Construction and Mantenance Costs. The consultants will review and update estimates of
unit costs for construction and maintenance works to be used in the modelling phase (HDM
and transport model), based on existing studies (e.g., Sharma and Shrestha (1992)) and data
from a sample of upgrading and construction works carried out in the recen past. Costs
should be considered both in financial as well as economic terms.
(c)   Road    . The consultant will review the reference system developed by MRCU. The
data for paved roads is described in Appendix 1. Road data for unpaved roads will be
limited to roughness, CBR of the naural subgrade, thickness and characteristics of the
pavement materials, road geometry and the carriageway and formation widths. It will be
collected on sample basis for typical sections and estimated elsewhere. The samling rate
will depend on the economic importance of the roads considered.
(d) Bridges. The consultants wil update the inventory of bridges done by MRCU. The
imventory will include a summauy description and classification into major types and
conditions of all bridges and ferries on the strategic road network, and suspension bridges
under MOWTADOR and MLD. A review of unit maienan  costs for bridges will also
be included.



- 86 -
Annex 7
Page 4 of 8
10. Rural Transvort Network. The consultants will review and update the following technical data:
(a)   Construction and Maintenance Costs.  The Consultants will review existing data in
construction, upgrading and maintaining district and rural roads, trails and suspension
bridges, taking into account risk engineering factors .
(b)  District Roads and Rural Roads. The technical data would be limited to simple condition
data for gravel roads (as described in Appendix 1), and reviewing the data and network
modelling of the District Road Network Analysis.
(c)   Trails and Suspion Bridges. The consultants will review the existing data from the
various trails and suspension bridges programs.
it. Air Transport Network. The consultants will review and update the basic data of air transport
network in order to esfimate basic unit costs to comnpare with other modes of transport affecting rural
accessibility.
Road Inventory and Database
12.  While the consultants will produce the road data described above primarily for the network
modelling, the format in which this information will be gathered and presented will be developed jointly
with the NPC and MOWT/DOR to develop a review process, preferably on a desktop computer. As part
of this effort, the consultants will review inventory procedures fromn DORJMRCU.
Analysis and Evaluation
13.  Strategic Road Network. The analysis would consist of a prioritization of constuction, upgrading
and maintenan   investments based on the network modelling, ranked according to economic criteria and
considering most liKely sectoral budget ceiling limitations.
14.  For analysis of the mintenance options of the existing roads of the strategic road network, the
consultants will group the referenced roads into categories or groups of homogenous sections with similar
characteritcs such as traffic range, pavement type and condition (roughness level), structural strength,
and so on. Sections requiring special attention should be analyzed separately. For all groups of road
sections defined above, the consultants will define the investments and the corresponding maintenance
needs that will be technically justified to match the capacity of the segment to future road transport
demand. The consultants will use HDM-i to compare the options and select the economically optimal
strategy for the full network. The options to be considered shall include construction of new road
sections and realigrnents; widening of existing pavements reconstmuction on existing alignments to
improved road standards (upgrading); provision of full strength pavement overlays (including widening
and levelling course where needed); periodic maintenane operations such as thin premLx overlays, single
or double surface levelling courses; improvement of subgrade stngth through better compaction methods
and/or stabilization; improvement of embankments and addition of drainage strcures to prevent damage
from flooding; and so on.
15.  RuraM Transport Netwok. The main objective would be to develop an Intgrted Rural Transport
Strategy (IRTPS) to develop paramets and criteria to prioritize rural transport investments in each



- 87 -
Annex 7
Page 5 of 8
distnct. The IRTPS would be a framework to assist local organiations in the planning of construction,
upgrading and mintnce of district and mral roads, trails and suspension bndges. Based on the global
demand assessments, the Consultants would assess the main human, financial and institutional resource
requirements to implement the strategy, and rather than identifying specific invetmens, it would
pnioritize in general terms the proposed investments of the District Road Network Analysis considering
to minimize total rural transport costs. The IRTPS could build upon the GIS application already
developed by NPC.
Priority Investment Plan
16. On the basis of the above data and analyses, the consultants will define the basic grid on which
the strategic road network should develop, an integated rural transport strateg (IRTPS), and the optimal
phasing of investments and maintenance over a 10-year period, indicatig the optil balance among:
i) consuction and upgrading of the strategic road network; (ii) rehabilitation and maintnance of existing
roads and bridges of the strategic network; (iii) constuction and upgrading of the rural transport network;
and (iv) rehabilitation and _mtenance of the exiting mral anspo network (includig suspension
bridges under MLD and MOWT). It is expected that the consutans duing the courwse of their work will
compare several strategies for the development of the strategic and rural transport networks, their
ntgration, and several manena    strategies, which take into account the specific physical and
economic conditions, likely enviro_nentl impacts and necessary mtgion measures and natural
constraints of Nepal relevant for road development such as, inter alia, the coutry's difficult topography,
limited absorptive capacity, scarcity of humn resources in the road sector, land use predomialy for
agriculture, ecologically/environmentally sensitive areas, and relatively uneven distbution of populaion
centers. The consutants will review the availability of local and foreign financing for the road subsector
in Nepal, make reasonable assumptions of fuure levels consistent with the lending programs of the major
donors in the subsector (ADB, IDA, Japan, SDC, UK ODA, etc.) and match these against the opdmized
programs in the 10-year Plan to be reviewed periodically.
17. On the basis of the PIP, the consultants will review detailed budgets for the remaining years of the
EPYP period (1992-1997).
M. TRAINW
18.  The consultants will conduct onthe-job training of staff of NPC and MOWTIDOR in data
coluection, network modelling and review of the PIP, and as necessary for MLD staff in dirict road
network prioritization.
19.  The consultants will assist the NPC and MOWT/DOR to identify,traning needs for HMG staff in
modern road planning and analytical techniques including GIS.  I
20. With a view to further developing the GIS capabilities at NPC, NPC and DOR will have complete
acoess to all computer software information and source code listigs and shall have the rights to distute
them withi the departm  or modify the source code accordigly.

IV. EQUIPMENT
18.  For carrying out the Technical Assistance, the consultans will procure vehicles and equipment
following the Bank's Guidelines on Procurement. On completion of their services, vehicles and
equipment will be handed over to the DOR.
V. STAEIN_q
19.  The consultants are expected to seek association with local consulting firms to provide the services
required. It is envisaged that a suitable mix of internationally and locally recruited professionals and
support staff will be needed in the fields of highway engineering, transport and agricultural economics
and planning, rural appraisal, pavement and materials engineering, bridge engineering, hydrology,
systems analysis and modelling, and other related fields.
VI. TIME SCHEDULE AND REPORTING REOUIREMENTS

of the contract. They shall prepare and submit the following report to HMG/N and IDA within the time

APPENDIX 1
Data to be collected for strategic road network from MRCU and field surveys:
(i)    Pavement
a.   Width. The consultants will measure pavement and shoulder widths as needed
but at least once in every kilometer.
b.   Curvature and Gradient. Using a hand held conmpass with a clinometer, or
other suitable instruments, the consultants will determine the average curvature
n degrees per km and the average gradient in mlan for each road section
referenced in the network.
C.   Structures and CBRs. The consultants will open test pits in the existing
pavements and shoulders every 25 km or less, if required, in order to
determine the existing pavement structure, measure thicknesses of each
individual layer, determine bearing capacity and neasure CBR and moisture
content of the natural subgrades and shoulders. Between two test pits, the
dynamic Cone Penetrometer (DCP) will be used on the shoulders to assess the
variability of CBRs of the subgrades and on the pavement layers (if driving
the DCP through the macadam layers is not found possible, then the
consultants will bore a pre-hole through it) to assess the pavement thickness
and bearing capacity profile and to determine the structural strength of the
pavements. DCP on pavements should be at 2 km intervals for national paved
highways and roads with more than 500 vpd and at 5 km intervals on other
paved roads. The consultant will ensure that deflection data (section (iii)
below) is available for some of the sites selected for the DCP.
(ii)    Rouighne. The consultants will review the roughness data from MRCU and if
required use a vehicle mounted response-type road roughness measunng system to
coUect additional data on existing roughness on each of the referenced road sections.
The system will be calibrated and periodically checked in accordance with the
Intnational Roughness Index (RI) standard. Consultants may limit the number of
runs per section to one in each direction unless the existng conditions warrant
oterwise.
(iii)    ltegtjon. The consultants will review the data collected by N.D. Lea International
and Roughton and Partners, and if required, measure additional existing pavement
deflections under a standard 8 T axle. All measurements will be carried out when
the pavements are soaked, preferably about the end of the monsoon, or else the
measures values should be adjusted suitably to represent soaked conditions. The
deflecion values will be correlated to the structural numbers determined from the
Dynamic Cone Penometr (DCP) measrements made under section (i) c above.



- 90 -
Am=N 7
Page 8 of 8
(iv)    Pavement Condition. The Consultants will review the Pavement Condition survey
for 1,000 kn carried out by MRCU, and if required, carry out additional surveys
using the same methodology, at a sampling rate of 5 per cent (50 meters in every one
kilometer), to detennine the surface distress (measured by dhe Surface Distress Index)
and the dominant mode(s) of distress (e.g., cracking, potholing, ravelling and rutting)
in each of the referenced road section.

ROAD MAINTENAN-CE AND REMABILITAURAN PROJECT
Economlic Evaluation
A.    IokWuctlon
1.           The civil works of the Project include:
1)    rehabiiion of strategic highways;
2)    rehailitation of strategic feeder roads;
3)    periodic maienance, including resealing and regrarveling of the strategic
network; and
4)    pilot labor-based distrct road mainte e.
2.           The economic analysis of the proposed project focusses on the civil work components.
The economic analyses are based on 1993 fincial costs, modified by appropriate conversion fcts to
represe  the economic costs. The rehabiLtation of strategic highways and feeder roads, as well as the
periodic mainte      of the strategic network are evaluated with the World Bank's Highway Design and
Maintenance Model (HDM M). In the case of the pilot labor-based maince projects, the World
Bank approved producer's surplus approach was used for the analysis.
3.           The cost of the civil works of the four compone   (including 10 percent physical
contingency and 8 pcent constution supervision) are estimated to be NRs 1,457 million. The economic
evaluation results indicate that the project will yield a high economic rate of return. The Economic Rate
of Retun (EIRR) vary from 11 to 36 percent. The economic cost the civil works is estimated to be NRs
1,311 million. The project will yield a Net Present Value (NPV) of NRs 1,414 million at 10 percent
social discount rate. The overall EIRR of the project is estimated to be 21.4 percent. The economic
indicators for various components and cash flows are listed in the following two tables:

4.           It should be highlighted here that the economic evaluation of the strategic feeder road
component was based on limited information. During negotiations, agreement was reached on the need
for a detailed feasibility study to be reviewed by IDA before undertaking these road works during Project
implementation. The feasibility studies will also include an environmental screening according to the
SEA. Only those projects meeting the following criteria would be financed under the Project:
1)    further economic feasibility studies demonstrate the project will yield an
economic rate of return equal to or higher than opportunity cost of capital in
Nepal, which is 10 percent;
2)    sound technical design are prepared; and
3)    an enviromnental screening show that the project will have no adverse impact on
the environment.
B.    Mai Benefits and Beneficiaries
5.           The major quantifiable benefits of the project include: (a) decrease in vehicle operating
cost through provision of better road surface condition; (b) decrease future major pavement rehabilitation
cost through provision of new surface layers to prevent further surface deterioration; (c) decrease in road
emergency maiWntenance costs due to the improvement in drainage and slope stabilization; and (d) decrease
in road user cost due to reduction in road closure when better drainage and slope stability are provided
by the program.
6.           Benefits from the project would accrue to road users in the form of transport cost and
time savings, and to the DOR in the form of reduced maintenance costs. The project will improve the
accessibility of the roads during the monsoon season improving the accessibility along the main roads.
The decrease in vehicle operation costs will reduce total freight transport costs and improve the overall
productivity of the regions.
C.    E-conomic Analysis with HDM Model
7.           Methodology. The evaluations of the proposed rehabilitation of strategic highways and
feeder roads, resealing and regravelling of strategic roads periodic maintenance) were prepared based
on the Bank's Highway Design and Maintnance Model (HDM m). The HDM-M is designed to estimate
total life-cycle costs - construction, maintenance and road user costs - as a function of road design,
maintenance standards, and other policy options. The model enables decision makers to compare costs
and benefits associated with different maintenance strategies.
8.           For any road, given the existing conditions of that road, the traffic characteristics, and
the mantenance policies, the HDM model can simulate the road surface conditions and vehicle operating
costs as they are affected by the level of maintenance. For each year of analysis, the road surface
conditions (roughness, rutting, cracking, etc.) are computed as a function of the pavement conditions at
the end of the previous year, pavement characteristics, climate, intensity of road maintenance, and both
traffic volume and composition. Vehicle operating costs on that surface are then computed as a function
of road surface conditions and vehicle characteristics. This simulation, repeated for each year of the
analysis period, produces the annual costs of road maitenance and vehicle operations for each



- 133 -
Annex 11
Page 4 of 13
maintenance policy, from which economic comparison can be made.
9.           Different vehicle operating cost functions can be adopted in HDM-IIl. Since most of the
country's vehicle fleet consists of Indian-made vehicles, the India vehicle operating cost functions were
adopted with some modifications to overcome problems with their predictions or to allow them to be
extrapolated to high levels of roughness. Four representative vehicles were selected including: (a)
passenger car (i.e., four cylinder engine, e.g., Toyota Corolla), (b) utility vehicle (i.e., four wheel drive
and pickup truck), (c) medium truck (i.e., 7-15 ton capacity, e.g., Tata SE 1210 with a high sided body),
and (d) buses (e.g., Tata LP/52). The summary of HDM-i input data is in the table below:
