10.          Preparation of Basic Inut Data. The basic input data required for the HDM model
consisted of:
(a)   existing road characteristics;
(b)    vehicle characteristics and costs;
(c)   traffic volumes, compositions and growth characteristics; and
(d)    maintenance unit costs.
11.          Existing road conditions include data on climate, road geometry, the type and strength
of the pavement structure, and existing surface roughness. The detailed road condition data are obtained
through field surveys conducted by DOR and various consultants teams.
12.          Based on the four representative vehicles, five vehicle types were considered in the
analyses. These vehicle types are listed as follows:
a) car
b) utility
c) empty truck
d) loaded truck
e) bus
13.          Traffic data include traffic volume, vehicle composition and growth characteristics.
Although the traffic composition varies from one road to another road, for simplicity, a standard traffic
composition derived from traffic surveys is used for all the subprojects in the analysis. Road traffic was
projected using a growth factor common to all vehicle types. An annual growth rate of 6 percent are used
in the traffic projection. The percentages of each vehicle type in ADT are listed as follows:
Vehicle Type              Percentage
Car                        15.0
Utility                    18.0
Empty truck               22.5
Loaded truck               22.5
Bus                        22.0
14.          Calibraion of HDM-lU. The calibration of the model was performed to ensure that the
predictions of HDM-lu were relevant to Nepal vehicles and operating conditions.
15.          The VOC are predicted mostly using the India VOC model since, as stated above, most
of the heavy traffic in Nepal are Indian produced vehicles. Speeds are predicted using the Brazil speed
prediction model but using India parameter values.
16.          HDM-M was modified to consider crack progression as a fimetion of traffic. However,
it was found that the surface treatment predictions were unreasonable so it was necessary to revert to the
standard time based equations. The HDM-HI equation for predicting the effect of overlays on roughness
was replaced with one derived in Indonesia on similar pavements to thuse in Nepal. The rate of crack



- 135 -
Annex 
Page 6 of 13
progression after an overlay was increased as a fimcdon of the amount of wide cracking before the
overlay.
17.          The caliron focused on the effect of roughness on VOC and not on the absolute values
of the VOC predictions. However, in conparing the predictions of HDM-M Nepal to truck data
collected in a small survey it was found that there was good agreement between the predicted and
observed costs. Thus, HDM-l Nepal should be appropriate not only for maintenance studies but also
for plaming purposes.
18.          Main           Policies. A road maintea   policy defines two aspecs: (i) the types of
mntenance ativity underken, and (ii) the intervals at which the activity should be performed. Various
types of maintenance activities are considered in the modeing. These activities and their unit cost are
listed as follows:
Maint. Type               F. Cost       E. Cost             Unit

19.          It should be noted that the above unit mantenance costs are for those activities listed only.
The actual cost of iial tratm  of the projects in the proposed road maintenance program will be
highe bcaue ther are usually other works involved, such as drainage clearing, minor slope
stabilization, etc.
20.          The tming of mantenance activities can be specified to follow a fixed schedule (e.g.
every 30 days, or every 10,000 vehicle passes), or to be condition responsive (e.g. patch all cracks when
crakng coves 20 percent of the pavement surface). For each sub-project, both a with-project and a
without-project case were established for analysis and compro n by the HDM model. To avoid
distorton, adequate maintea    levels were assumed for both "with" and "without" cases.
21.          Since routine mainten  are not commonly practiced in Nepal, for all the projects in



- 136 -
Anx 11
Page 7 of 13
regravelling, no maintenance was selected while for paved road only patching the potholes are selected
for "without" cases.
22.          For rehabilitation or resealing projects, "with" cases adopt the following maintenance
policy:
(a)   resealing or overlay immediately;
(b)    patching 100 percent of pothole area and resealing every 7 years.
23.          For gravelling and sealing projects, the following maintenace policies was adopted for
"with" case in the model:
(a)   regravellirg and sealing with a surface dressing (SBST) immediately
(b)   resurfacing whenever the surface course is worn out to restore the thickness of
the surface course to the original thickness.
D.    Ma1i Anulvsl for Perodic M             Proar
24.          A large  mber of road sections were initially identified to be included in the periodic
reseang program. A field surv  to collect data on traffic, pavement surface distress and road roughness
for each road section was conducted by DOR. In order to prioritize those roads which are more likely
to yield high economic rates of retun, an initial screening of roads was conducted excluding those
projects which appeared to have low economic rates of returns.
25.          The projects' screening was carried out with an HDM matrix analysis in which roads
were classified according to their ADT, roughness (IRI) and surface distress index (SDI). An analysis
with HDM was carried out for the coreponding mntenace and representative ADT, lIR and SDI for
each group. A net present value matrix was built based on the results of the HDM analyses. The projects
in matrix cell with negative NPV would be classified as unfeasible and were therefore excluded from the
program The order of tratment priorities was also derived, and served as the basis to generate annual
pacages. The two tables below show the NPV (per kIn) and orders of treaunent priorities under different

26.          Results of Economic Evaluation of Reabitaina Stategc HihwM. There are six
projects included in the rehabfilaton of sategic highways component with a total distance of 320
kilometers. The major naitenance activities inchlde bituminous overlay, resealing, drainage and slope
stabilization. This cmont is esmated to cost NRs 651 million (in financial terms, NRs 586 million
in economic terms). The projects will yield overall NPV of NRs 553 million at 10 percent social discount
rate. EMRR's vary from 12 to 42 percent with an overall value of 19.4 percen. The detailed results are
presented in Table I(a pd). The overall cash flow for this cponentspresented in Table 2
(appendix).
27.          Rests of Econmic EvaluationOfRebiHtang Strag  Feeder Roads. Seven project
were originaUlly ideatified to be included in the program. Economic analysis indicates that four of them
would have low economic rates of retun. Therefore only three roads are included in this component,
namely Birgunj-Kalaiya, Tansen-Rid}-Thamgs and Nepalgunj-Bagoudar, with a total distance of 133 km.

The rehabilitation activities includes regravelling of gravel road, graveling an earth road, widening and
bridge repairing. The financial cost of this component is estimated to Nrs 210 million (Nrs 189 million
in economic terms). The EIRR's vary between 10 to 11 percent. The component will yield a NPV of Nrs
4.6 million at 10 percent social discount rate. The overall EIRR for the component is estimated to be
10.4 percent. The detailed results are presented in Table 3 (appendix). The economic cost-benefit cash
flow are presented in Table 4 (appendix). The evaluation of strategic feeder roads rehabilitation was
prepared on the basis of limited data. During negotiations it was agreed that a detailed feasibility study
be carried out for each of the projects in this component for review by IDA during the Project.
28.          Results of Economic Evaluation of Periodic Maintenance. Out of 2,000 kilometer of the
core strategic road network, about 40 road sections passed the initial screening. Further analysis revealed
that 8 road sections will yield an EIRR lower than 10%, the opportunity capital cost in Nepal. These
road sections were therefore excluded from the program. The remaining progran includes 32 road
sections with a total distance of 716 kilometers. Of these, about 650 kilometers were selected for the IDA
fnanced component of the RMRP, including 460 kilometers proposed for resealing of the existing paved
surfamce, and 150 kilometers for gravelling and sealing. The summary and the detailed list of the projects
included are presented in Tables 5, 6, and 7 (appendix).
29.          The financial costs of each road section were estimated based on the unit maintenance
rates. The basic unit rates were initially developed by the local consultants. They were estimated on a
per kilometer basis. These rates were then adjusted based on the road surface conditions. The basic unit
maintenance costs are listed as follows:
Unit Costs ('000 NRs/kn)

30.          The total cost of the component is estimated to be Nrs 571 million (financial cost, NRs
514 in economic terms). The EIRR's for this component fluctuate between 10 and over 50 percent. The
overall NPV of the component is estimated to be NRs 826 million. The EIRR is estimated to be 27.3
percent. The detailed cost and benefits of various road sections are presented in Table 6 (appendix). The
economic benefits and costs cash flow for the entire components are presented in Table 7 (appendix).

prepared by Multi Disciplinary Consultants Ltd.. During the preparation of the economic analysis, the
consultant conducted a socio-economic survey with interviews of a large number of households in the
zones of influence of the roads. Origin-Destination (OD) surveys were also carried out. The estimated

34.           he economic benefits of the project are from savings in transport cost and likely increase
in farmer production and productivity due to better access to the market. It is estimated that the economic
cost of the civil components of the project would amount to Nrs 23 million (Nrs 27 million in financial
terms). The project will yield a NPV of NRs 30 million at 10 percent social discount rate. The economic
rates for each of the road sections inchlded in the project are all above 26 percent. The overall EIRR for
this components is esmaed to be 36 percent. The detailed costs and benefits for the projects are as

35.          Sensitivity analyses were carried out for road rehabilitation and periodic maintenance
components. The results indicate that the project will remain viable if the construction cost rose by 10
percent and benefits dropped by the same proportion, reducing the overall EIRR to 20 percent. It also
indicates that under this scenario, the EIRR of rehabilitating Strategic Feeder Roads drops to 9 percent.
Since the analysis for this components was based limited infonnation and only vehicle operating cost
saving was counted as benefit, the actual EIRR for this conponent will probably higher than the above
figure and likely to be above the acceptable level of 10%. The detailed results of sensitivity analysis are
presented in Tables 8 through 11 (appendix).

ROAD MAINTENA   AN) EHABITATION POJEC
Implemention of the Envomnt  As             ent
1.            This Annex is presented in two parts. The first part contains a compilation of suggestions made
by the Bank and the DOR for expansion and darificadon of the draft Sectoral Environmental Asesment of the
Road Maintenance and Redabilitation Project. Tle second section of the Annex addresses institutional srtening
measures that will need to be adopted if the DOR is to implement the SEA in an effective manner.
PART 1: Sectoal Environmental Aessment
hnpact Aswssment and Mamement
2.            The IDA environmental scening of this project places it in Category 'B'. This category suggests
that  'low-grade'  environmenal impacts may reut from project constuction and/or operaton.  Some
environmenal asesme, less than a conplete enviromental impact assessment, is required. During preparation
of the Project it was determined that a Sectral Enviro_nental Assessment (SEA) was a suitable response to the
minor environmeal problems that may or may not be present as a result of project implementadon. The SEA is
meant as a general guide to envimental as ment of sbprojects included under the IDA's proposed Road
Maintenance and Rehbilitation Project. It does not cover new road consution or peent a comprehensive
covrage of environmeta impact management of road constuction acdvides or operation, although it is anticipated
that it could be expanded in the future to include these other project types. The following sections reflect the
agreements reached during Project appraisal based on DOR's review and comments, and the recomnendations have
been taken into account durmg the prparon of the fin  SEA report.
Pres _inz and Impact A      _m M  u nt_ of the SA
3.            The SEA itsdf generaly provides a satisfactory methodology for handling environmental
management issues within the road mantenance and rehabilitationprogram of the IDA's proposed Road Maintenance
and Rehabilitation Project. I presents a simple screenig process for identifyng those subpojects that may require
special envi_rmenta review, and covers impact assessment and impact management of all other subprojects in an
activity by activity basis. However, it will eventually need s_tegthing if it is to be applied to major road capacity
expansion or new road projects, especialy those in the mountainu section of the country. While this was not
included in the consultant's terms of rehrence for ppration of the SEA, the SEA should contain a section on
Next Steps'. This section should outline the addito   work needed for preparation of more detailed guidelines
for environme  nal        of constuctin   and operation of road projects in Nepal (to serve as the basis of a
TOR for additional environmental wok in the fAure).
4.            The SEA methodology developed and used in other Asian countries also includes 'pre-screening'
criteria which should be used at the project identification and prioritizaton stage. These criteria are designed to
eliminme those subprojects from a credit AT TIE PROJECT IDENTIFICATION STAGE that would be likely to
be incompatible with IDA's envionmet Operationa Directivs or stated policy guidelines. For example, road
projects of any kind in national pars, World Heritage listed areas, water supply watersheds, or through habitats
coaining rare or endanged plants and wildlife would generaly not be eligible for IDA finance. Projects of this
nature should never reach the stage of being included in an SEA, as it is -ery unliky that the IDA or the Bank
would finance them. The SEA should be expanded to include a 're-screening* process to be implemented at the
project ideniicaion and prioritizadon stage of the project cycle.



- 161 -
Page 2 of 6
S.            The screening process included in the SEA divides subprojects in the Road Ma _eac and
Rehabilitation Project into those that require a project specific environmental assessment and those that can be
generally assessed under the impact assessment and impact managmt provisions included in the SEA. The SEA
exprssly excludes assessment of environmental impacts of the environment on the various subprojects. However,
the DOR has suggested that the screening process may be able to be modified to include IDENTIFICATION of
subprojects that would benefit from bioengineering to mimize probems related to slope stability. If this can be
done in a general way using slope, vegetation cover and land slip frequency on existing roads in the area, then it
is suggested that the screening process be so modified.
6.            The project specific environmental assessment proposed for subprojects with more significant and
likely impacts is an Initial Environmental Evaluation (LEE). The MEE is a recognized envirment assessment tool.
However, there is not a single, universally agreed upon, format for an lEE amongst workers in the envir
management field. indeed, there are many proposed formats. The IDA's Environmental Assessment Operational
Directive (OD 4.01) does not specifically mention or provide guidance for preparation or evaluation of an EE, nor
does it pesnt a list of project activities or project types that would require preaation of an IEE. In fact, the term
IE is note ven used in the Directive. It is therefore essentW that the SEA include a recommended foinat, or at
least r    eons for scope and content, of an IEE that would be suitable for project-specific en *viroent
assesmt of a road maintenance or road rehabilitation subproject under the Credit.
Environmental  naement Comonent of the SEA

does not mean that all options pre,nted need to be used. It only means that the DOR should be provided with a
relatively comprehensive collection of options from which a suitable subset of mag measures could be

Duties of an Environmental Unit in the DOR
12.           The responsibilities of the Environmental Unit could include:
I)     Environmentll liaison, training and support to the Divisional Engineer Offices of DOR, and to the
District Development Committee Eigineering Offices (on an "as needed" basis);
2)     Preparation, in cooperation with the Environmental Divisionof the National Planning Commission,
and periodic updating of guidelines for impact assessment, impact management and environmental
monitoring/supervision of road and bridge projects;
3)     Project Seenig as required under the SEA and as may be required in the future once
comprehensive (including all types of road projects) screening guidelines have been prepared and adopted;
4)     Preparation of any Initial Environmental Assessments that may be needed for the IDA's proposed
Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Ptoject, or perhaps in the future for any other project regardless of
whether or not it is funded by IDA or the Bank;
5)     Preparaton of consultant Terms of Reference for any Environmental Assessments (EA);
6)     Supervision and review of EAs prepared for road projects implemented by DOR;
7)     Securig clearance of EAs for DOR projects through EA presentation to the newly created and
empowered Enviromen  Council and/or donor (such as the IDA or the Bank);
8)     Monitoring of project Envionmental Management Plans to ensure that management measures are
being implemented as directed, and that the specified measures are as effective as originally thought. It
may be usefl to link this Department-level role with the corresponding need to monitor envuinental
impact of construction and operation of District Roads, which are under the control of the Distict
Development Committees;
9)     Post project environmental review.
13.           At present, there appear to be two assistant engineers within the Environmental Unit of the DOR,
one responsible for environental aspects and the other addressing bioengineeritg needs of the DOR. The
Divisional Engieerng position as head of the Environmental Unit is curffently being covered by the head of the
Planning Deparmet. All members of the Environenl Unit have been assigned responsibilities other than those
directly related to their envnmental duties. During Project appraisal concem regarding the need to have a full
time staff assigned to implement the SEA; the requirements of implementiAg the SEA for the Road Maintenance
and Rehabilitation Project (much less the other environmental duties menioned in paragrah 9) cannot be adequately
met by staff who are allocated to these duties on a part-time basis.
14.           This issue was highlighted in the SEA, which rcmmended that at least one full-time
environmental staff be appointed to the Enviomental Unit. As the Environmental Unit already exists, and because
there is already one person filling an assistant engineermg position in this Department, IDA believes that this
conditon has already been met. What is lacking is clear delegation of authority to the Environmental Unit.
15.           The staff respondble for environmental matters, and Indeed the department itself, needs a
more clearly deied structural progam and Ust of dudes within the Department. The suggestions presented
in pragraph 9, above, could be used to form the basis of a deted catalog of Department reponsibilies,

Environmental Unit Staff Develonment
16.           After clearly identifying the role of the Environmental Unit and the duties of the environmental
management specialist within it, it is essential that the Department and its staff be given any needed technical skills
and equipment. The Department is not currently able to implement the environmental management requirements
of the Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project, as presented in the SEA. It is therefore necessary to include
equipment, technical assistance and training components within the credit so that the DOR can adequately implement
the SEA recommendations. The training and technical assistance components needed would include:
I)     Tranina:  A two to three month, concentrated training program in environmental impact
assessment for the person designated by DOR as its full-time enviromnental impact assessment specialist.
The lack of such a program in Nepal will require the DOR environmental specialist to be sent overseas.
One suitable program exists at the University of Aberdeen in Scotland and others that may be suitable could
be identified before Credit effectiveness. This training should be the main priority of envirornmental
strengthening component in the Credit and should be undertaken as soon as possible;
2)     Short-term Exuatri   Assistanee: The development of an IEE process and more detailed
guidelines for environmental (including social) impact management of road and bridge projects in Nepal.
The newly created Environmental Protection Committee and acceptance of National Environmental
Assessment Guidelines (recently passed by the Parliament) require each sectoral Ministry to prepare
guidelines in cooperation with the Environmental Division of the National Planning Commission (NPC).
This process has already been used to prepare sectoral guidelines for Forestry and Industry, and transport
has recently been prepared in draft form. It is suggested that upon the return of the DOR environmental
specialist from overseas training, an internationai expert on environmental management of road and bridge
impacts be retained for a period of atxroximatelv 3 months to assist in the further developing the nuidelines
and hel, the DOR Environmental Unit and the Environmental Division of NPC to start imWlementation.
The guidelines for EE to be prepared would expand upon and develop a more appropriate sectoral
orientation to the IEE matrix presented in the National Environmental Impact Assessment Guidelines of
1992. It would also present detailed description of the information required before the EE could be
completed. Likewise, the road and bridge envionmental guidelines would expand on the SEA, present
a more detailed project screening (including new, and more extensive rehabilitation and betterment projects)
process, a more detailed guide to road construction activities and related environmental impacts,
construction supervision and long term environmental monitoring methodologies for road projects, etc.
These expanded guidelines would concentrate on work in upland and mountainous areas of Nepal, and
would be written for use primarily by Divisional Engineers and DOR Project Managers;
3)     Divisional and Deuartmental Semioars: As necessary, once the IEE, road and bridge
environmental guidelines had been prepared, reviewed and adopted by DOR, the Ministry of Works and
Transport and the Environmental Protection Committee, six Regional seminars (one in each of Nepal's 6
Regions) should be held to present and explain the guidelines to Divisional Engineers and Project Managers
of the DOR. When the seminar is held in the Central Division- which contains Kahmandu- members of
the DOR should participate. Funding of the proposed seminars as presented in the attached table includes
an allowance to permit participation in the seminars by the Enviomnental Division of the NPC;
4)    PIriodIc LocalIExDa e Assisance: Finally, as explained in the SEA, specialist consultant
advice should be periodicatly made available to the Envronmenl Unit of DOR. The SEA suggests that
approximately 1 month per year over the life of the loan would be sufficient, and the consultant assistance
could come from either within or outside of Nepal. Topics for these one month consultancies should be
left open at present, as it is not certain exactly what will be required in the future. However, it does seem
essential that the first of these short consultancies should address traffic safety. It is anticipated that the



- 165 -
Annex 13
Page 6 of 6
assistance on traffic safety would not be required until at least the second year after loan effectiveness and
would generally involve review of the current status of road safety in Nepal. It should also identify any
gaps that may be present in the staff skills, equipment, guidelines for use and placement of road furniture,
institutional organization, etc, that relates to the handling of road safety in Nepal.
17.           The DOR receives considerable assistance in the "environmental sector" from the British Overseas
Development Administration (ODA). At present, this mainly deals with bioengineering, but may be expanded in
the future to include road safety and other areas of environmea management. Any consultancy assistance to the
DOR that originates from the IDA Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project must be coordinated with the
existing and budgeted ODA activities in DOR and other relevant government agencies in Nepal.
Iwlementation of the SEA by the Conractor
18.           The FIDIC guidelines specify that the contractor must prepare a plan for submission to the client
that details how environmental management measures specified in the contract (usually in only a general manner)
are to be implemented. It is expected that the DOR will apply this condition to contractors working on the Road
Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project.
19.           Since it is very unlikely that contractors chosen for work on subprojects under the Credit will have
either sufficient knowledge of envirnmental management of road projects, or of the environment in which work
is to be undetaken, it is essential that a staff from the DOR Environmental Unit be seconded to each relevant
contractor (the SEA should provide guidance here) for a period of approximately I month to help in the preparation
of this environmental management implementation plan.
Eciument and Other Assistance to the Environmental Unit of DOR
20.           The subprojects under the Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project are scattered throughout
Nepal. It is essental that the Environmental Unit staff have ready and reliable access to transport. The consultant
that is to assist the Departmen with the preparation of road and bridge, and IEE guidelines will also need transport
during his 3 month stay in Nepal. It is proposed that the Credit inclue purchase of a 4-wheel drive vehicle for use
by the consultant, which would then be turned over to the DOR Environmental Unit upon completion of his/her
assignment.
21.           The Credit should also include funding for books, periodicals and references relevant to
environmental management and monitoring of road infrastructure projects.

INTRODUCION
. His Majesty's Govermnent of Nepal (HMG) is presently undertaking preparation activities for
a proposed Road Maintenance and Rehabilitation Project (RMRP), for which it is
expected that fumding will be provided by the International Development Association
(IDA) and other donors. The purpose of the proposed project is to address part of the
backlog mainan  and rehabilitation on Nepal's core network of highways and feeder
roads.
2.    One of the componenu of the proposed RMRP is to support the Deparutnet of Roads (DOR) by
implementing an operational audit of rehabilitation and maintnance operations in selected
highways and feeder roads.
3.    As part of these activities, HMG requires consultant services to cany-out an operational audit and
deliver an opinion report based on an audit of rehabilitation, periodic, emergency, and
routine maintenance activities implemented by contract on a sample of roads.
OBJECTIVES

a.    carry-out an operational audit of DOR's maience operations by contract, including
rehabilitation, periodic, emergency and routine maintenance operations in FY 1994/95;
b     review in detail the following DOR operations: budgeting, supervision and cost control
and reporting of maintenance operations; and
c.    deliver an operational audit report on road maintenance.

ROAD CONSMTUCTION AND MAINTEANCE MATERIALS STUDY
His Majesty's Government (HMG) of Nepal has requested a credit from the inteaonal
Development Association (IDA) towars the cost of a proposed Road Maintance and Rilitation
Project (RMRP) . It is intended that partof the proceeds from the credit be aplied to eligible paymen
under the consultancy services described below.
The main objective of the Study is to:
i)    Review current practice and Nepal Road Stdards relbed to:
roads and pavement materials;
bridges and bridge materials;
road and bridge geometry, technical specifications and sample forms of
conact;
ii)    Coorduia  and review on-going work on road and bridge standards which is being
undeaken by the  _Mtennce Rehabilitation Coordnution Unit (MRCU) in the
Deparent of Roads (DOR) aimed at defining, comparing and remmendng
appropriate, improved, cost effective design stndards and specifications which give
specia atention to makig maximum use of locally available materials and local
onstruction, rehabilitation and maitnan  techiques;
ii)   Inventory loca materis which can be economically and effectively used in road and
bridge con ction, rehabilitation and ma ance  following exisig and expected
revised standards;
iii)   Propose road pavemt and bridge construction, rehabilitation and maintenance
techniques which make optimal use of local materials and minimize costs;
iv)    Suggest materil related standards for road and bridge consruction and maintenance;
v)    Prepare technictl manuals guidelines for road and bridge constmuion, rehabilitation and
vi)    Review current materil testing procedures undaken as part of road pavement
gthinhdlitation and bridge rehabilitation and suggest adequate procedures
based on local materials; and
vii) Based on the resolts of the study, suggest modifications to the standard specifications for
roads and bridges and the sample bidding docaments for the Road Maintenance and
Rehabilitation Project, including provisions for the Terai (plains), hills and monwins.

2.    SCOPE OF CONSULTANCY SERVICES
2.1    Gemal
The Consultant shall perform all engineering, fmancial analysis and related work as described
herein to attain the objectives of the Study and shall maintain close liaiscJ with the Deparnment
of Roads (DOR).
The DOR will provide the consultant with all relevant data and reports available but the
Consultant shall be entirely responsible for checking their correctness and for the analysis and
interpretation of all information received and for fte rmmndations and conclusions in their
report submissions.
2.2    Pta Review
The Consultant will collect and review available data on the DOR road network including, inter
alia
i)    current, prevalent and expected revised design standards for roads (pavement and
geometric) and bridges;
ii)    current technical specifications for constuton and maintenance of roads and bridges;
iii)   road and bridge rehabilitation design reports;
iv)   reports on road construction industry (e.g., N.D. Lea International, East Consult,
Contractor Association of Nepal and others);
v)    reports on local materials and soils;
vi)   existing data collected by various consultant teams (i.e., N.D. Lea International,
Roughton and Partners, Scott, Wilson and Kirkpatrick and MRCU) to include road
pavement condition surveys comprising visual condition (i.e., surface distress, drainage
and traffic ranges), roughness and deflection, CBR values on subgrades and borrow
materials, test pitting and sample testing of subbase, base and surfacing, including
thickness and CBR values;
vii) traffic and axle load surveys;
viii)bridge condition surveys and reports (including Bridge
Mangeme  System, Road Flood Rebabilitation Bridge Condition Surveys).
2.3       irfentorv of  M
i)    The Consultant will inventory road pavement and bridge materials currently available in
Nepal or which can be economically importod and which have potential for effective and
economic use in road and bridge construction, rehabilitation and maintenance;
The materias are to be classified according to their fundamental behavior into various
categories with different classes according to their strength chrcteristics;



- 172 -
Annex 16
Page 3 of 6
iV0    For each type of material the Consultant will list and map the main existing, known and
potential quarries for road construction purposes, sites or points of production and specify
their location, average anmnal production, potential production and reserves and establish
a unit cost. The Consultant will comment on the efficiency of production and the
feasibility of improvement. The Consultant will differentiate between terai (plains), hill
and mountain regions. If no reliable information exists on either the quality or the
quantity available the Consultant will collect samples, carry out such investigations,
surveys and laboratory testing as required for this evaluation;
iii)   This work will be carried out in two phases. In the first phase the Consultant will list
the data already available and prepare a program of site investigation and laboratory
study for the second phase with an estimate of the cost. With the approval of the DOR
the second phase will be implemented and the results incorporated in the inventory;
2.4 Road ftons#
2.4.1  AsesMt of Road  avkement Cosrction. Rebabilitation and Maintenance Tecaues
i)    The Consultant will define the pavement construction, rehabilitation and maintenance
techniques (improved subgrade, sub-base, base and surfacing layers) which have been
proved and used in Nepal, their results and suggestions regarding techniques that have
not been adopted but could make econonic sense if developed and adapted to Nepal's
conditions, taking into account the specia conditions prevailing in the three regions
including the Terai, hills and mountains;
ii)    For each of the techniques the Consultant shall compare the potential performance of road
and bridge construction materials, cost (including life cycle cost), advantages and
disadvantages, considering the implications for the use of equipment-baied or labor-based
technologies during construction and maintenance;
iii)   From the most cost effective techniques and with the approval of the DOR, the
Consultant will select for further study at least three techniques for improved subgrade,
four techniques for subbase and base and four techniques for surfacing. For each
technique in three different regions the Consultant will prepare and carry out a study
program of sample collection and field and laboratory tests which shall include, but not
be limited to, the following:
a)    for improved subgrade materils - soaked CBR values (at four days) and
unsoaked CBR values at three compaction levels and variable moisture content;
b)    for materials stabilized with lime, cement, hydrocarbon binders (bitumen, etc) -
compressive strength and stability;
c)    for mecbanically stabilized materials - particle size analysis, plasticity,
compaction and CBR values;
d)    for surfacing materials - (including crushed aggregate and river gravel available
in diferent regions) particle size analysis, plasticity, aggregate shape, size,
adsorption, abrasion, adhesion and cmshing strength, Marshall stability, flow and
air-voids, all as appropriate;
The consultant may dispense with some laboratory tests by a bibliographical reference

available.

may employ other local laboratories but the consultant will be entirely responsible for the
procedure and rests of such tests;

i)    The Consultant will review the existng road classification and will in coordnaton with
MRCU adopt a road classification and geometric standards in vanous categories, defined
on the basis of importance traffic volume and consted standard, for three regions
including the Terai, hills and mountains;
i)    The Consultant will carry out the following for each category of road:
a)    selection of parameters (e.g. trffic flow conditions, low volume nuning speed,
vehicle operating cost, comfort and safety, clearances, permanence of the road
link impact on the region served) to define service levels and study their variation
range;
b)    defie target service levels per category;
c)    sdection of basic design paramet  (e.g. design speed, width of cross section
lements, crossll and superelevation, geometry of vehicles, vehicle dynamic and
opration paramets) and study their vanation range;
d)    from the service levels and basic design parameter data deduce and define
minirmu values for horizontl and vertical alignment, intersections and
clearances;
iii) The Consultant will then develop a road construction materials' guidelines which would
be considered by DOR at the time that MRCU would recommend a set of geomeic
design standards for roads and bndges based on least economic cost (vehicle opertng
cost plus construction and mintenance cost).
